The Second Claim: Emanation by Nature, Not by Choice
Ghazali presents the second philosophical claim and its sun analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second philosophical position that Ghazali refutes: that principles generate events necessarily by nature, not by deliberate choice, and how the variety of effects is attributed to the differing preparedness of receptacles.

Core Idea
Philosophers claim the universe flows from God by necessity like light from the sun.

Ghazali now turns to a second group of philosophers who agree that events come from higher principles but differ on the mode. They argue that the principles act by necessity and nature, not by will and deliberation. To illustrate, they compare the emanation of the universe to sunlight, where the diversity of effects is explained by the different capacities of the receiving objects. This lesson unpacks that analogy and its implications for divine action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice how Ghazali frames the second position (المقام الثانى) and the key terms used.
The passage is a continuous exposition, so read it as a whole.
2
Identify the core claim of the second position
Extract the main assertion: that principles produce things 'necessarily and by nature' (باللزوم والطبع), not by deliberation or choice, like the sun emitting light.
This is the key point of contrast with the Ash'ari view of voluntary action.
3
Analyze the sun analogy and the role of preparedness
Ghazali lists various examples (polished body, dense body, air, stone, etc.) to show how a single principle (the sun) produces different effects based on the preparedness (استعداد) of the receptacle. Understand that the philosophers use this to account for diversity without invoking choice.
Note the term استعداد المحل and its plural استعدادات.
4
Connect to the broader debate on divine attributes
Reflect on why Ghazali is discussing this: it undermines divine will and attributes of action. The philosophers reduce God to a necessary cause, stripping Him of choice.
This lesson sets up Ghazali's refutation in subsequent sections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثانى : مع من يسلم أن هذه الحوادث ، تفيض من مبادئ الحوادث ، ولكن الاستعداد لقبول الصور ، يحصل بهذه الأسباب المشاهدة الحاضرة؛ إلا أن تلك المبادئ أيضاً ، تصدر الأشياء منها ، باللزوم والطبع لا على سبيل التروى والاختيار صدور النور من الشمس ، وإنما افترقت المحال في القبول ، لاختلاف استعدادها ، فإن الجسم الصقيل ، يقبل شعاع الشمس ويرده حتى يستضيء به موضع آخر ، والمدر لا يقبل ، والهواء لا يمنع نفوذ نوره ، والحجر يمنع ، وبعض الأشياء يلين بالشمس ، وبعضها يتصلب ، وبعضها يبيض ، كثوب القصاب ، وبعضها يسود ، كوجهه ، والمبدأ واحد ، والآثار مختلفة ، لاختلاف الاستعدادات في المحل.
The second claim: with those who concede that these events flow from the principles of events, but the preparedness to accept the forms occurs through these observed present causes; except that those principles also generate things from themselves necessarily and by nature, not by deliberation and choice, like the emanation of light from the sun. However, the receptacles differ in acceptance due to the difference in their preparedness. For example, a polished body receives the ray of the sun and reflects it so that another place becomes illuminated, a dense body does not receive it, air does not prevent the penetration of its light, stone prevents it, some things soften in the sun, some harden, some turn white like a washerman's garment, some turn black like his face. The principle is one, but the effects are different due to the difference of the preparations in the receptacle.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of a second position (المقام الثانى) held by some philosophers. They agree that events flow from higher principles but argue that the immediate preparedness for receiving forms is determined by observable causes. However, they maintain that these principles act by necessity and nature (باللزوم والطبع), not by deliberation or choice, analogous to how light emanates from the sun. Ghazali then explains that the diversity of effects from a single principle is due to the differing preparedness (استعداد) of the receptive substrates (محال). He provides examples: a polished object reflects light, a dense object blocks it, air transmits it, stone blocks it; the sun causes some things to soften, others to harden, some to whiten, others to blacken. The principle (المبدأ) is one, but the effects vary according to the differing preparations (استعدادات) in the receptacle.

Key Terms
المقام الثانى
باللزوم والطبع
استعداد المحل
الجسم الصقيل
المدر
الاستعدادات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that a single cause cannot produce different effects without the cause itself changing.
Correction: Ghazali shows that the philosophers account for diversity by pointing to the different preparedness (استعداد) of the recipients, not by any change in the cause.

Checkpoint
According to the second claim, why do different objects react differently to the sun?
A) The sun changes its nature for each object
B) The objects have different inherent capacities (استعدادات)
C) Different angels are assigned to each object
D) The sun is not the true cause

Key Takeaway
The second philosophical claim reduces divine action to a necessary, natural emanation, analogous to sunlight, with diversity explained solely by the preparedness of the recipients.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a blessing or a difficulty, do you instinctively attribute it to natural causes (like preparation or chance) rather than to the deliberate will of Allah? How would your gratitude or patience change if you truly believed that every event flows from His free choice, not from necessity?
?
The philosophers claim that God acts like the sun—unable to refrain from radiating existence. Do you ever feel as though Allah's mercy or justice is automatic, something you can rely on without turning to Him? What would it mean for your relationship with Him to know that He chooses to give, not because He must, but because He wills?
?
If God's acts were necessary, there would be no room for prayer or supplication to change anything. How deeply do you believe that your du'a can alter what would otherwise be 'necessary'? What does your prayer life reveal about your true belief in God's free will and personal involvement?

The Philosophers' Doctrine of Emanation from the Giver of Forms
Ghazali reports the philosophers' consensus that all phenomena emanate from the Giver of Forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali reports the philosophers' agreement that all phenomena emanate from the Giver of Forms, and why this sets up his critique of natural causality.

Core Idea
Natural objects are mere preparations; the true agent is the Giver of Forms.

In this passage, Ghazali presents a key doctrine of the philosophers: that events like seeing colors do not come from the sun, eye, or object, but from a separate immaterial principle called the Giver of Forms. This is not Ghazali's own view but a position he will use to question the philosophers' consistency. As you read, notice how the philosophers shift agency from physical objects to an angelic source.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the consensus
The text begins by stating that the reliable philosophers agreed on a certain point. Note that this is a consensus among them, not a fringe view.
2
Identify the specific example
The philosophers use the example of colors impressing in the eye. Identify the three conditions they mention: sun, healthy eye, colored body. They call these preparations (muhayyi'at).
3
Understand the generalization
The philosophers then extend this to every event. This is a universal claim.
4
See the implication
Ghazali draws the conclusion: this doctrine invalidates the claim that fire, bread, medicine are true agents. They are merely preparatory.
5
Prepare for Ghazali's critique
This passage sets up Ghazali's argument that the philosophers themselves undermine natural causality, which will lead to his occasionalist critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا اتفق محققوهم، على أن هذه الأعراض والحوادث، التي تحصل عند وقوع الملاقاة بين الأجسام. وعلى الجملة عند اختلاف نسبها، إنما تفيض من عند واهب الصور، وهو ملك من الملائكة، حتى قالوا : انطباع صور الألوان في العين يحصل من جهة واهب الصور، وإنما طلوع الشمس والحدقة السليمة، والجسم المتلون، معدات ومهيئات، لقبول المحل هذه الصور، وطردوا هذا في كل حادث · وبهذا تبطل دعوى من يدعى، أن النار هي الفاعلة للإحراق، والخبز هو الفاعل للشبع، والدواء هو الفاعل للصحة، إلى غير ذلك من الأسباب .
And for this reason, their reliable scholars agreed that these accidents and events that occur at the moment of contact between bodies, and in general when their relations differ, only emanate from the Giver of Forms (Wahib al-Suwar), who is an angel among the angels, even to the point that they said: the impression of the forms of colors in the eye occurs from the direction of the Giver of Forms, and the rising of the sun, the healthy eye, and the colored body are merely preparations and dispositions for the receptacle to accept these forms. And they extended this to every event. And by this, the claim of those who claim that fire is the agent of burning, bread is the agent of satiety, medicine is the agent of health, and so on from among the causes, is invalidated.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage reports the consensus of the reliable philosophers (muhaqqiqūhum) that all sensory phenomena—such as the impression of colors in the eye—do not issue from the natural objects (sun, eye, body) but from a separate immaterial principle called the Giver of Forms (Wahib al-Suwar), identified as an angel. The sun, eye, and colored body are only preparatory conditions (muʿiddāt wa muhayyiʾāt) that dispose the receptacle to receive the forms from that giver. This principle is then generalized to every event. Ghazali then draws a conclusion: this doctrine refutes the naïve claim that fire, bread, or medicine are true agents (fāʿila) of burning, satiety, or health. By the philosophers' own admission, the real agent is something else. This sets up Ghazali's occasionalist argument that only God creates effects.

Key Terms
واهب الصور
فيض
أعراض
حوادث
ملاقاة
معدات
مهيئات
فاعلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: You might think Ghazali is endorsing the philosophers' view, but he is reporting it as a premise that he will later use against them.
Correction: Ghazali is not agreeing with the emanation doctrine; he is presenting it as a philosophical position that ultimately undermines natural causality, which he will refute.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers reported by Ghazali, what is the true source of the impression of colors in the eye?
A) The sun
B) The healthy eye
C) The Giver of Forms
D) The colored body

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' own doctrine of emanation from the Giver of Forms undermines the idea that natural objects are true causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever act as if physical causes (wealth, effort, medicine) are the real agents in your life, forgetting that the true Giver is above them? What would change in your daily reliance if you truly believed that all effects come directly from God?
?
When you experience a blessing (health, success, safety), do your thoughts immediately race to the secondary causes (doctor, hard work, security) or do they first turn to the Creator? How can you train your heart to see the Giver behind every gift?
?
Ghazali exposes that even the philosophers admitted physical things are mere preparations. Yet you might still live as though your own actions and natural laws are the real causes. What is one area of your life where you can practically shift from feeling like the agent to feeling like a receptacle, waiting for God's emanation?

Causes Act by Nature: The Philosophers' Second Position
Ghazali presents the philosophers' second position: causes act by natural necessity like the sun.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that causes act by natural necessity, not by choice, and how they use the sun analogy to illustrate this determinism.

Core Idea
Causes emanate like the sun: necessarily, not by choice.

In this passage, Ghazali presents the second philosophical position: those who accept that events emanate from principles but insist that these principles act by necessity, not will. The sun's light is used as an analogy: it shines necessarily, and differing effects are due to the receiver's preparedness, not the cause's choice. This sets up the determinist worldview that Ghazali will later critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice how Ghazali presents the philosophers' claim and their key analogy.
Focus on the phrase 'like the emanation of light from the sun'.
2
Identify the analogy and its parts
The philosophers compare the cause (e.g., fire) to the sun. The sun emits light necessarily; different objects react differently due to their preparedness. Find the list of objects and their reactions.
The sun is the principle; objects are the receivers.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The philosophers conclude that if two identical objects meet the same cause under identical conditions, the effect must be the same. Ask yourself: does this argument prove that causation is necessary?
This conclusion denies the possibility of miracles, like the preservation of Abraham in fire.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثانى : مع من يسلم أن هذه الحوادث ، تفيض من مبادئ الحوادث ، ولكن الاستعداد لقبول الصور ، يحصل بهذه الأسباب المشاهدة الحاضرة ؛ إلا أن تلك المبادئ أيضاً ، تصدر الأشياء منها ، باللزوم والطبع لا على سبيل التروى والاختيار صدور النور من الشمس ، وإنما افترقت المحال في القبول ، لاختلاف استعدادها ، فإن الجسم الصقيل ، يقبل شعاع الشمس ويرده حتى يستضيء به موضع آخر ، والمدر لا يقبل ، والهواء لا يمنع نفوذ نوره ، والحجر يمنع ، وبعض الأشياء يلين بالشمس ، وبعضها يتصلب ، وبعضها يبيض ، كثوب القصاب ، وبعضها يسود ، كوجهه ، والمبدأ واحد ، والآثار مختلفة ، لاختلاف الاستعدادات في المحل . فكذا مبادئ الوجود فياضة ، بما هو صادر منها ، لا منع عندها ، ولا بخل ، وإنما التقصير من القوابل . وإذا كان كذلك ، فمهما فرضنا النار بصفتها ، وفرضنا قطنتين متماثلتين ، لاقتا النار على وتيرة واحدة ، فكيف يتصور أن تحترق إحداهما دون الأخرى وليس ثم اختيار .
The second position: with one who concedes that these events emanate from the principles of events, but that the preparedness to accept the forms occurs by means of these observed present causes; except that those principles also cause things to proceed from them by necessary concomitance and by nature, not by deliberation and choice, like the emanation of light from the sun. And the loci (places of reception) only differ in acceptance due to the difference of their preparedness. For the polished body receives the sun's ray and reflects it so that another place is illuminated by it, while the opaque does not accept, and the air does not prevent the penetration of its light, and the stone prevents, and some things soften by the sun, some harden, some whiten like the cloth of the dyer, some blacken like his face—while the principle is one, and the effects differ due to the difference of preparations in the receptacle. Thus the principles of existence are effusive with what proceeds from them; there is no prevention or stinginess on their part; the shortcoming is only from the receivers. And if this is so, then whenever we suppose fire with its property, and we suppose two identical pieces of cotton coming into contact with the fire in the same manner, how could it be conceived that one burns and the other does not, when there is no choice?
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents the second position (al-maqām al-thānī) of the philosophers. They concede that events emanate from principles (mabādiʾ al-ḥawādith), but maintain that the immediate causes (al-asbāb al-mushāhada al-ḥāḍira) serve as preparations for reception. However, the principles themselves act by necessary concomitance (al-luzūm) and nature (al-ṭabʿ), not by deliberation and choice. The analogy of the sun is used: the sun's light emanates necessarily, and different objects react differently due to their varying preparedness (istiʿdād). The sun does not choose. Similarly, the cosmic principles are generous (fayyāḍa), giving what they have without stinginess; any failure is in the receivers. From this, they conclude that if two identical objects (e.g., cotton) contact the same cause (fire) under identical conditions, the effect must be identical if there is no choice. This argument is used to deny the possibility of miracles like the preservation of Abraham in the fire.

Key Terms
طبع
لزوم
اختيار
استعداد
قابل
فياض
مبدأ
ملاقاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers claim the sun has a will like a human.
Correction: They use the sun as an example of natural, non-voluntary causation; the sun does not 'choose' to shine.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' second position, why do different objects react differently to the same cause?
A) The cause chooses different effects
B) The cause changes its nature
C) The receivers have different preparedness
D) Random chance

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that causes act by necessity, not choice, using the sun as a model; effects differ due to the receiver's preparedness, not the cause's will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a trial or a missed opportunity, do you immediately look for a 'natural' cause to blame (e.g., your own lack of effort), or do you ask whether God chose this for a wisdom you may not see? How would your heart respond if you truly believed that every outcome is a direct act of God's will, not just a natural consequence?
?
The philosophers claim that causes are generous and never withhold. But when you pray for something and it does not happen, do you feel that God is holding back from you? Reflect: Could it be that you are the unprepared receiver? What would it mean for you to prepare your heart to receive what God gives, rather than demanding He give according to your plans?
?
If causes acted by necessity like the sun, then identical conditions would always produce identical results. But your life is full of surprises—moments of sudden mercy or unexpected hardship. Have you ever insisted that things 'should' have gone a certain way because the causes seemed the same? How can you cultivate a faith that expects God to act freely, even when the apparent causes are identical?

Ghazali's First Answer to the Denial of Ibrahim's Miracle
Ghazali reports the philosophers' denial of Ibrahim's miracle and gives his first counterargument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's first argument that the philosophers' denial of Ibrahim's miracle is based on a false assumption of necessary causation, and that God acts by will, not by necessity.

Core Idea
God's will is the true cause; natural objects are merely occasions.

The philosophers deny Ibrahim's miracle by arguing that if fire remains fire, it must burn. Ghazali's first response is to challenge the assumption of necessary causation. He recalls his earlier refutation and affirms that God creates burning by His will. He then faces an objection that this leads to chaos. This passage presents the first step of his rebuttal and the beginning of that objection.

Guided Path
1
Understand the philosophers' denial
Read the first part where Ghazali reports the philosophers' argument: Ibrahim cannot be unharmed in fire unless the fire's heat is removed or Ibrahim's nature changes, both impossible. Note their assumption that fire necessarily burns.
2
Identify Ghazali's first response
Ghazali says: 'We do not concede that causes act necessarily.' He refers to his earlier proof that God creates by will. This is the core of his answer.
He says they already refuted necessary causation in the discussion on the world's temporal origination.
3
Examine the objection
An opponent objects: if causation is not necessary, then anything could happen—beasts unseen, books turning into youths, etc. This objection challenges the coherence of Ghazali's view.
The passage ends abruptly; note that this is just the beginning of the objection.
4
Reflect on the stakes
Consider why the objection is serious: it points to the problem of induction and the regularity of nature. How might Ghazali respond? Think about the role of God's habitual patterns (ʿāda).
This sets up Ghazali's second answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا المعنى أنكروا وقوع إبراهيم - صلوات الله عليه وسلامه ـ في ومن النار ، مع عدم الاحتراق ، وبقاء النار ناراً ، وزعموا : أن ذلك لا يمكن ، إلا بسلب الحرارة من النار ، وذلك يخرجها عن كونها ناراً ؛ أو بقلب ذات إبراهيم - عليه السلام - ورده حجراً ، أو شيئاً لا تؤثر فيه النار ، ولا هذا ممكن ولا ذاك ممكن . والجواب له مسلكان : الأول ، أن نقول : لا نسلم أن المبادئ ، ليست تفعل بالاختيار ، وأن الله تعالى لا يفعل بالإرادة ، وقد فرغنا من إبطال دعواهم ، في ذلك ، في مسألة حدوث العالم ، وإذا ثبت أن الفاعل يخلق الاحتراق بإرادته ، عند ملاقاة القطنة النار ، أمكن فى العقل ، ألا يخلق الاحتراق ، مع وجود الملاقاة . فإن قيل : فهذا يجر إلى ارتكاب محالات شنيعة ، فإنه إذا أنكر لزوم 6 المسببات ، عن أسبابها ، وأضيفت إلى إرادة مخترعها ، ولم يكن للإرادة أيضاً منهج مخصوص معين ، بل أمكن تفننه ، وتنوعه ، فليجوز كل واحد منا ، أن يكون بين يديه ، سباع ضارية ، وزيران مشتعلة ، وجبال راسية ، وأعداء مستعدة بالأسلحة لقتله ، وهو لا يراها ، لأن الله تعالى ، ليس يخلق الرؤية له . ومن وضع كتاباً في بيته ، فليجوز أن يكون قد انقلب عند رجوعه إلى بيته ، غلاماً أمرد ، عاقلا متصرفاً ، أو انقلب حيواناً . ولو ترك غلاماً فى بيته، فليجوز انقلابه كلباً ، أو ترك الرماد ، فليجوز
This meaning they denied: the occurrence of Ibrahim—peace and blessings of Allah be upon him—in the fire without being burned, with the fire remaining fire. And they claimed that this is not possible unless by removal of heat from the fire, which would take it out of being fire, or by transforming the essence of Ibrahim—peace be upon him—and turning him into stone or something fire cannot affect. Neither is possible. The answer has two approaches: The first: that we say: we do not concede that the principles do not act by choice, and that Allah Most High does not act by will. We have already finished refuting their claims in that regard in the question of the temporal origination of the world. And if it is established that the Agent (al-Fa'il) creates burning by His will at the contact of the cotton with the fire, it is rationally possible that He does not create burning despite the existence of contact. If it is said: This leads to committing monstrous absurdities. For if the necessary connection between causes and effects is denied, and everything is referred to the will of the Creator, and the will also has no specific fixed pattern but can be diverse and varied, then it would be possible for each of us that there be in front of him ferocious beasts, blazing fires, towering mountains, and enemies armed with weapons ready to kill him, yet he does not see them, because Allah does not create vision for him. And whoever puts a book in his house, it would be possible that when he returns to his house, it has turned into a beardless, intelligent, active youth, or turned into an animal. And if he left a boy in his house, it would be possible that he turn into a dog, or if he left ashes, it would be possible...
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' denial of the miracle of Ibrahim (AS) and Ghazali's first response. The philosophers argue that for the miracle to occur, either the fire's heat must be removed (which would change its nature) or Ibrahim's substance must be changed (making him fire-proof). Both options are impossible, so the miracle cannot happen. Ghazali's first answer (al-maslak al-awwal) is to reject their premise that causes act necessarily. He reminds that he has already refuted their claim of necessary causation in the discussion on the world's temporal origination. Instead, Allah creates effects by His will. Therefore, when cotton contacts fire, Allah can create burning or not. Then an objection (fa-in qīla) is raised: if necessary causation is denied, then the world becomes chaotic—anything could happen, such as beasts or fires being present but unseen, or books turning into youths, etc. This objection is not fully answered in this passage; Ghazali's complete response would continue. The passage thus presents the first step of Ghazali's rebuttal and sets up the objection he will address.

Key Terms
اختيار
إرادة
فاعل
خلق
موجب
طبع
معجزة
إبراهيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ghazali denies all causality and randomness governs everything.
Correction: Ghazali affirms that the true cause is Allah's will, and He typically follows a consistent pattern (ʿāda), so the world is not random. The objection here is meant to challenge that view.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, why is it impossible for Ibrahim to be unharmed in the fire?
A) Because fire cannot exist without heat
B) Because Ibrahim was not a prophet
C) Because miracles cannot happen
D) Because the philosophers did not believe in Ibrahim

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's first answer is that the philosophers' denial of miracles assumes a necessary causal connection that does not exist; God creates effects by His will, so He can create a fire that does not burn.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where natural outcomes seem inevitable—like a sickness with no cure or a hopeless financial crisis—do you truly believe that Allah can change the outcome by His will? How does this belief affect your dua and reliance on Him? Will you live today as if Allah alone is the true cause of every effect?
?
The philosophers thought miracles were impossible because they assumed necessary causation. Have you ever limited Allah's power by assuming that certain things must happen according to physical laws? What would change in your life if you truly believed that Allah creates effects directly, without any necessity from the created thing?
?
Ghazali argues that the real Agent (al-Fa'il) is Allah. In your daily life, do you attribute success to your own efforts, talents, or natural processes, or to Allah's will? How can you cultivate a deeper awareness of His direct involvement in every moment, from the beating of your heart to the next breath you take?

The Philosophers' Necessary Causality
Ghazali presents the philosophers' view on causality with the cotton ball example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' deterministic view of causality and how Ghazali uses the cotton ball example to highlight a contradiction.

Core Idea
The philosophers' causal necessity leaves no room for miracles.

Ghazali presents the philosophers' claim that the principles of existence are generous and act without choice. He then tests this claim with the example of two identical cotton balls and fire. This sets the stage for his critique: if causation is necessary, how can miracles occur?

Guided Path
1
Understand the philosophers' claim
Read the first sentence: the principles of existence are overflowing, no prevention or stinginess. The deficiency lies in the receivers.
This is the philosophers' doctrine, not Ghazali's own view.
2
Examine the cotton ball example
Consider two identical cotton balls contacting the same fire in the same way. According to the philosophers' principles, both must burn equally because there is no choice.
3
Identify the implication
If causality is necessary, then miracles (like the fire being cool for Ibrahim) are impossible. Ghazali will use this to argue against the philosophers.
Think about how this example challenges the philosophers' system.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فكذا مبادئ الوجود فياضة ، بما هو صادر منها ، لا منع عندها ، ولا بخل ، وإنما التقصير من القوابل . وإذا كان كذلك ، فمهما فرضنا النار بصفتها ، وفرضنا قطنتين متماثلتين ، لاقتا النار على وتيرة واحدة ، فكيف يتصور أن تحترق إحداهما دون الأخرى وليس ثم اختيار .
Thus the principles of existence are overflowing, by virtue of what emanates from them; there is no prevention with them, nor stinginess; rather the deficiency is from the receivers. And if it is so, then whenever we assume fire with its property, and we assume two identical cotton balls, both contacting the fire in the same manner, how can it be conceived that one of them burns without the other when there is no choice?
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' deterministic view of causality. They hold that the principles (i.e., celestial or intellectual causes) emanate existence generously without any will or choice. Deficiency arises from the receivers (the material substrates), not from the giver. Ghazali then illustrates this with the example of two identical cotton balls contacting fire identically. According to the philosophers' own framework, since there is no choice or will in the fire or the principles, the effect (burning) must occur uniformly. Thus it is inconceivable that one burns and the other does not. This sets up the problem: the philosophers cannot account for the miracle of Ibrahim (AS) without violating their own principles.

مبادئ
فياضة
قوابل
اختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage represents Ghazali's own belief about causality.
Correction: This is Ghazali presenting the philosophers' position, which he will later critique.

Checkpoint

B) Because the principles act by necessity without choice
C) Because the cotton balls are different
D) Because God wills it

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' necessary causality cannot explain why miracles occur, as Ghazali will argue.

The Philosophers' Skepticism Objection
The philosophers object that denying necessary causality leads to total skepticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objection that denying necessary causality leads to total skepticism, and recognize that this is the philosophers' argument, not Ghazali's position.

Core Idea
Denying causality leads to absurd skepticism about everyday things.

In this passage, the philosophers present a powerful counterargument against Ghazali's denial of necessary causal connections. They argue that if causation is not necessary and God's will is arbitrary, then we can never be certain about anything—leading to laughable absurdities. This objection challenges the very possibility of knowledge about the physical world.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' objection
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that this is an objection (فإن قيل) and not Ghazali's own view.
The phrase 'فإن قيل' signals an objection from the opponent.
2
Identify the absurd scenarios
The philosophers list many scenarios that would become possible if causality is not necessary. Scan the passage and note at least three examples they give.
Examples include books turning into boys, water turning into apple trees, etc.
3
Understand the conclusion
The philosophers conclude that if such possibilities are allowed, we must hesitate about everything. They claim this proves the denial of causality leads to total skepticism.
The key phrase: 'فلا بد من التردد فيه' (hesitation is necessary).
4
Reflect on the rhetorical strategy
Consider why the philosophers use such extreme examples. Their aim is to show that denying causality makes the world unintelligible and undermines all empirical knowledge.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فهذا يجر إلى ارتكاب محالات شنيعة ، فإنه إذا أنكر لزوم المسببات عن أسبابها ، وأضيفت إلى إرادة مخترعها ، ولم يكن للإرادة أيضاً منهج مخصوص معين ، بل أمكن تفننه ، وتنوعه ، فليجوز كل واحد منا ، أن يكون بين يديه ، سباع ضارية ، وزيران مشتعلة ، وجبال راسية ، وأعداء مستعدة بالأسلحة لقتله ، وهو لا يراها ، لأن الله تعالى ، ليس يخلق الرؤية له . ومن وضع كتاباً في بيته ، فليجوز أن يكون قد انقلب عند رجوعه إلى بيته ، غلاماً أمرد ، عاقلا متصرفاً ، أو انقلب حيواناً . ولو ترك غلاماً فى بيته، فليجوز انقلابه كلباً ، أو ترك الرماد ، فليجوز انقلابه مسكاً ، وانقلاب الحجر ذهباً ، والذهب حجراً ، وإذا سئل عن شيء من هذا ، فينبغى أن يقول : لا أدرى ما فى البيت الآن، وإنما القدر الذي أعلمه أنى تركت في البيت كتاباً ، ولعله الآن فرس ، قد لطخ بيت الكتب ببوله وروثه ، وإنى تركت في البيت جرة من الماء ، ولعلها الآن انقلبت شجرة تفاح فإن الله تعالى ، قادر على كل شيء ، وليس من ضرورة الفرس ، أن يُخلق النطفة ، ولا من ضرورة الشجرة ، أن تخلق من البذر ، بل ليس من ضرورة الشجرة ، أن تخلق من شيء ، فَلَعَلَّهُ خَلَقَ أَشْيَاءَ لَمْ يَكُنْ لَهَا وُجُودٌ مِنْ قَبْلُ ، بَلْ إِذَا نَظَرَ إِلَى إِنْسَانٍ لَمْ يَرَهُ إِلَّا الْآنَ ، وَقِيلَ لَهُ : هَلْ هَذَا مَوْلُودٌ ؟ ! فَلْيَتَرَدَّدْ ، وَلْيَقُلْ يَحْتَمِلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ بَعْضُ الْفَوَاكِهِ فِي السُّوقِ قَدْ انْقَلَبَ إِنْسَاناً ذَلِكَ الْإِنْسَانُ ، فَإِنَّ اللَّهَ تَعَالَى قَادِرٌ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ مُمْكِنٍ ، وَهَذَا مُمْكِنٌ، فَلَابُدَّ مِنَ التَّرَدُّدِ فِيهِ ، وَهَذَا فَنٌّ يَتَّسِعُ الْمَجَالُ فِي تَصْوِيرِهِ ، وَهَذَا الْقَدْرُ كَافٍ فِيهِ .
If it is said: This leads to the commission of ugly absurdities. For if the entailment of effects upon their causes is denied, and they are attributed to the will of their inventor, and the will also has no specific fixed course, but rather variation and diversification are possible, then each of us may allow that there are in front of him ferocious beasts, blazing fires, firm mountains, and enemies armed with weapons to kill him, yet he does not see them because God does not create vision for him. And whoever placed a book in his house may allow that when he returns to his house it has turned into a beardless, rational, self-acting boy, or turned into an animal. And if he left a boy in his house, he may allow that he turns into a dog; or if he left ashes, he may allow that they turn into musk; and stone turns into gold, and gold into stone. And if he is asked about something of this sort, he ought to say: I do not know what is in the house now; the only thing I know is that I left a book in the house, and perhaps it is now a horse that has soiled the library with its urine and dung; and I left a jar of water in the house, and perhaps it has now turned into an apple tree. For God is able to do all things, and it is not necessary that a horse be created from sperm, nor that a tree be created from a seed, rather it is not necessary that a tree be created from anything; perhaps He created things that did not exist before. Rather, if he looks at a man he has never seen before, and it is said to him: Is this one born? Then let him hesitate, and let him say: It is possible that some fruit in the market has turned into that man. For God is able to do every possible thing, and this is possible; thus hesitation is necessary. This is a topic that widens the scope in its portrayal, and this amount suffices in it.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is the philosophers' objection to Ghazali's first approach. They argue that if effects are not necessarily linked to their causes but rather depend on God's will which can vary arbitrarily, then no certain knowledge about the world is possible. They list many absurd scenarios: we might be in danger without seeing it; objects might transform arbitrarily (books into boys, ashes into musk, etc.); we can never be sure of anything. The philosophers claim that this skepticism is unacceptable and thus the denial of causality must be false. The passage ends with Ghazali noting that this is a broad topic but this much is sufficient.

Two Sources of Certainty for Possible Events
Editor explains Ghazali's two sources of certainty for possible events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali explains our ability to have certain knowledge about the non-occurrence of possible events, either through divine creation or habitual custom.

Core Idea
Certainty about ordinary events comes from divine creation or ingrained custom, not from impossibility.

The objection of skepticism suggests that if events are merely possible, we can never know whether they occur. Ghazali's response, as clarified here, shows that this objection misunderstands how knowledge works. The passage explains two concrete ways we can be certain that an event did not happen, even though it could have.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the two ways Ghazali mentions.
The passage is a response to a skeptical objection.
2
Identify the two sources of knowledge
Locate the two methods (divine creation and custom) and notice how each provides certainty.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this preserves both divine power and ordinary human knowledge.
This connects to the broader debate on causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أن نقول : إن ثبت أن الممكن كونه لا يجوز أن يخلق . يعنى لو كان الشيء الممكن الوجود - كانقلاب الكتاب فرساً - إذا كان غائباً عنا، يجعلنا في حيرة من أمره، ولا ندرى هل وقع، أم لم يقع؟! لزمت هذه المحالات ... أما إذا كان في الإمكان، أن يحصل لنا علم، بأن هذا الانقلاب – رغم إمكانه – غير واقع وغير حاصل، لم تلزم هذه المحالات وفى الإمكان أن يحصل لنا هذا العلم بأحد طريقين: ( ا ) أن يخلق الله فيها ابتداء، علماً بعدم حصول هذا الانقلاب، فنجزم بأنه – برغم إمكانه - غير حاصل، بمقتضى هذا العلم الذي خلقه الله فينا . (ب) أن جريان العادة، بعدم حصول هذا الانقلاب، يرسخ في أذهاننا - برغم إمكانه - العلم بعدم وقوعه .
And the answer: we say: if it is established that the possible thing cannot be created. That is, if the possible thing – such as a book turning into a horse – when it is absent from us, makes us in perplexity about its affair, and we do not know whether it occurred or not, then these absurdities would follow. But if it is possible for us to obtain knowledge that this transformation – despite its possibility – is not actual and does not occur, then these absurdities do not follow. And it is possible to obtain this knowledge in one of two ways: (A) That God creates in us initially knowledge of the non-occurrence of this transformation, so we are certain that – despite its possibility – it did not occur, by virtue of this knowledge that God created in us. (B) That the habit (custom) of the non-occurrence of this transformation becomes ingrained in our minds – despite its possibility – [providing] knowledge of its non-occurrence.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents the editor's clarification of Ghazali's response to the skepticism objection. The editor begins by quoting the beginning of Ghazali's answer, which is incomplete. He then explains that the absurdist examples only follow if we assume we have no way of knowing whether such transformations occur. However, Ghazali argues that we can possess knowledge that such events are not actually happening, even though they are possible. This knowledge can come from two sources: either God directly creates in us a certainty that the transformation did not happen, or the regular habitual course of events (custom) establishes in our minds the knowledge that such things do not occur. Thus, we are not left in perpetual doubt; rather, we have certain knowledge based either on divine intervention or on the established pattern of nature.

جريان العادة

The Skeptic's Challenge to Empirical Certainty
Ghazali presents the skeptic's objection that empirical knowledge is uncertain due to divine omnipotence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the skeptic uses divine omnipotence to undermine all empirical knowledge and why this is a serious challenge.

Core Idea
If God can do anything, nothing we haven't seen is certain.

In this passage, Ghazali presents a radical skeptical objection: if God is omnipotent, then any object could transform into any other at any time. The skeptic argues that this possibility destroys our certainty about the unseen world. Before we can defend knowledge, we must confront this challenge head-on.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the vivid examples: book turning into a horse, water into an apple tree.
These examples are meant to be absurd but logically possible.
2
Identify the core argument
The skeptic argues that since God can do any possible thing, any change is possible. Therefore, we cannot know what is happening in an unseen place.
The key is the phrase 'قادر على كل شيء ممكن' (powerful over every possible thing).
3
Consider the implications
If we accept this skepticism, we must respond with 'I do not know' to any claim about the unseen. The passage shows how this undermines all empirical knowledge.
This is not a denial of God's power but a challenge to certainty.
4
Reflect on the strength of the objection
Ghazali presents this as a serious challenge that must be answered. Ask yourself: how would you respond to this skeptic?
The rest of the Tahafut will provide responses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من انقلابه مسكاً ، وانقلاب الحجر ذهباً ، والذهب حجراً ، وإذا سئل عن شيء هذا ، فينبغى أن يقول : لا أدرى ما فى البيت الآن، وإنما القدر الذي أعلمه أنى تركت في البيت كتاباً ، ولعله الآن فرس ، قد لطخ بيت الكتب ببوله وروثه ، وإنى تركت في البيت جرة من الماء ، ولعلها الآن انقلبت شجرة تفاح فإن الله تعالى ، قادر على كل شيء ، وليس من ضرورة الفرس ، أن يُخلق النطفة ، ولا من ضرورة الشجرة ، أن تخلق من البذر ، بل ليس من ضرورة الشجرة ، أن تخلق من شيء ، بل لعله خلق أشياء لم يكن لها وجود من قبل ، بل إذا نظر إلى إنسان لم يره إلا الآن ، وقيل له : هل هذا مولود ؟ ! فليتردد ، وليقل يحتمل أن يكون بعض الفواكه في السوق قد انقلب إنساناً ذلك الإنسان ، فإن الله تعالى قادر على كل شيء ممكن ، وهذا ممكن، فلابد من التردد فيه ، وهذا فن يتسع المجال فى تصويره ، وهذا القدر كاف فيه .
From its turning into musk, and the turning of a stone into gold, and gold into stone, and if he is asked about something like this, he should say: I do not know what is in the house now. The only thing I know is that I left a book in the house, and perhaps now it is a horse that has defiled the library with its urine and dung. And I left a jar of water in the house, and perhaps it has now turned into an apple tree, for God Almighty is powerful over all things. It is not necessary that a horse be created from a sperm-drop, nor is it necessary that a tree be created from a seed; rather, it is not necessary that a tree be created from anything. Rather, perhaps He created things that did not exist before. If he looks at a person he has not seen until now, and it is said to him: Is this born? Let him hesitate and say: It is possible that some fruit in the market has turned into that human, for God Almighty is powerful over every possible thing, and this is possible. So he must hesitate about it. This is a topic that has wide scope for elaboration, and this much is sufficient.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents a skeptical objection that Ghazali attributes to the philosophers or a hypothetical opponent. The objector argues that if God is omnipotent, then any change is logically possible. For example, a book could turn into a horse, water into an apple tree, or a fruit into a human. Since we do not directly witness these transformations, we cannot be certain that they have not occurred. The passage describes how someone should respond with hesitation, saying 'I do not know' about any unseen state of affairs. The point is to show that if we take logical possibility alone, all empirical knowledge collapses. Ghazali presents this as a strong challenge that must be answered.

Ghazali's Answer: Divine and Customary Certainty
Ghazali's response: two sources of certainty about empirical possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's two grounds for empirical certainty—direct divine creation of knowledge and the force of habitual custom—and why they answer the skeptical objection without denying divine omnipotence.

Core Idea
God directly creates certainty, and habit cements it. Both are compatible with possibility.

Ghazali has just presented the skeptical objection: if empirical events are merely possible, we can never be certain they will occur as expected. Now he delivers his answer. Rather than grounding certainty in logical necessity, he points to two concrete sources: God's direct creation of knowledge in us, and the deep imprint of repeated custom. This passage shows how he preserves both human confidence and divine freedom.

Guided Path
1
Understand the hypothetical rebuttal
Ghazali begins by stating the implication of the skeptic's stance: if it were impossible for God to create knowledge of a possible event's non-occurrence, then absurdities would follow. But this is not the case.
The phrase 'إن ثبت أن' signals a conditional that he will reject.
2
Identify the first source: divine creation of knowledge
Ghazali affirms that God does create in us knowledge that certain possibilities are not actualized. This is direct and certain.
He says 'خلق لنا علماً' – God creates knowledge.
3
Identify the second source: habitual custom
Even without direct divine intervention, the continuous repetition of habitual patterns ('عادة') firmly implants in our minds the expectation that things will continue as before.
This 'tarseeḵ' (firm establishment) is psychological but reliable.
4
Connect to the larger argument
Notice that Ghazali does not claim these events are necessary; they remain possible. Certainty does not require necessity; it can come from God's creative act or from ingrained habit.
This refutes the skeptic's false dichotomy: either necessity or no certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : أن نقول : إن ثبت أن الممكن كونه ، لا يجوز أن يخلق للإنسان علم بعدم كونه ، لزم هذه المحالات ؛ ونحن لا نشك في هذه الصور التي أوردتموها ، فإن الله تعالى خلق لنا علماً ، بأن هذه الممكنات ، لم يفعلها ، ولم ندع أن هذه الأمور واجبة ، بل هي ممكنة ، يجوز أن تقع ، ويجوز أن لا تقع ، واستمرار العادة بها ، مرة بعد أخرى ، يرسخ في أذهاننا ، جريانها على وفق العادة الماضية ترسيخاً لا تنفك عنه
The answer is: we say: If it is established that a possible thing, it is not permissible that a person be created with knowledge of its non-existence, these impossibilities would follow. But we do not doubt these examples you have mentioned, for God Almighty has created for us knowledge that these possibilities He has not done. We do not claim that these matters are necessary; rather, they are possible, they may occur and may not occur. And the continuity of habit in them, time after time, firmly establishes in our minds that they proceed according to past custom, a firm establishment from which we cannot separate.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage gives Ghazali's direct refutation of the skeptical objection. He states that if it were impossible for a person to have knowledge that a logically possible event did not occur, then the skeptic's absurd conclusions would follow. But in fact, God directly creates in us knowledge that certain possibilities are not actualized. Additionally, the constant habitual repetition of events (custom) firmly fixes in our minds the expectation that they will continue as before. Thus we have certainty about empirical matters even though they are not logically necessary. Ghazali distinguishes two sources of certainty: direct divine creation of knowledge and the force of customary experience. This answer preserves both divine omnipotence and ordinary human certainty.

Key Terms
علم
عادة
ممكن
خلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali denies any real certainty about empirical matters because they are merely possible.
Correction: Ghazali affirms that we do have certainty, either through God's direct creation of knowledge or through the firm establishment of habitual custom.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what are the two ways we can have certainty about empirical facts despite their possibility?
A) Through logical demonstration and sense perception
B) Through direct divine creation of knowledge and habitual custom
C) Through consensus and tradition
D) Through revelation and reason

Key Takeaway
Empirical certainty is possible through God's direct creation of knowledge and through the firm imprint of habitual custom—both compatible with the contingency of all events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you have a deep-seated certainty that the sun will rise tomorrow? If so, does that certainty come more from habit or from a conscious trust in God's creation of knowledge? Which one do you actually rely on?
?
When your own carefully built habits fail you—like a daily routine that suddenly breaks—do you despair or do you still hold that God's knowledge of possibilities is enough? Examine your heart honestly.
?
Can you point to a single moment in your life where you felt God directly created in you a certainty about something that reason alone could not guarantee? If not, why has He not done that for you? If yes, why do you still seek proofs from custom?

Prophetic Knowledge of Possibles and Divine Power
Ghazali defends prophetic knowledge and divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that prophetic knowledge of contingent future events does not negate their possibility or limit divine power.

Core Idea
Knowing an event will not happen does not make it impossible.

In this passage from Tahafut al-Falasifa, Ghazali defends the possibility of prophetic knowledge against philosophers who claimed it would contradict divine omnipotence. He clarifies that knowledge of non-occurrence does not turn a possible event into a necessary one. This lesson will walk through Ghazali's reasoning step by step, showing how habit, divine creation, and possibility coexist.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to hear Ghazali's voice, then read the English translation. Focus on the logical flow: he starts with a conditional, then clarifies the nature of possibles, then applies it to prophets.
Notice how Ghazali uses 'rather' (بل) to pivot from his opponent's scenario to his own view.
2
Identify the key distinction
Identify Ghazali's key distinction: possible events remain possible even when their non-occurrence is known. Find where he explicitly states that possibility does not require actual occurrence.
3
Understand the role of habit ('āda)
Examine how Ghazali uses the concept of 'habit' (عادة) to explain how knowledge of non-occurrence becomes settled in the mind, but remains open to divine intervention.
Habit is not necessity; it can be broken by God.
4
Connect prophetic knowledge to divine power
See how Ghazali concludes that prophetic knowledge does not limit divine power. God knows that He will not do a possible act at a certain time, but He could if He willed.
This refutes the charge that prophetic knowledge would make God incapable of doing the known event.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للإنسان علم بعدم كونه، لزم هذه المحالات؛ ونحن لا نشك في هذه الصور التي أوردتموها، فإن الله تعالى خلق لنا علماً، بأن هذه الممكنات، لم يفعلها، ولم ندع أن هذه الأمور واجبة، بل هي ممكنة، يجوز أن تقع، ويجوز أن لا تقع، واستمرار العادة بها، مرة بعد أخرى، يرسخ في أذهاننا، جريانها على وفق العادة الماضية ترسيخاً لا تنفك عنه. بل يجوز أن يعلم نبي من الأنبياء - عليهم السلام - بالطرق التي ذكر وها، أن فلاناً لا يقدم من سفره غداً، وقدومه ممكن، ولكن يعلم عدم وقوع ذلك الممكن، بل كما ينظر إلى العامى، فيعلم أنه ليس يعلم الغيب، في أمر من الأمور، ولا يدرك المعقولات، من غير تعليم، ومع ذلك فلا ينكر أن تقوى نفسه وحدسه، بحيث يدرك ما يدركه الأنبياء - صلوات الله وسلامه عليهم - على ما اعترفوا بإمكانه، ولكن يعلمون أن ذلك الممكن لم يقع، فإن خرق الله العادة، بإيقاعها فى زمان، تخرق العادات فيها، انسلت هذه العلوم عن القلوب، ولم يخلقها فلا مانع إذن من أن يكون الشيء ممكناً، في مقدورات الله تعالى، ويكون قد جرى في سابق علمه، أنه لا يفعله مع إمكانه، في بعض الأوقات، ويخلق لنا العلم بأنه ليس يفعله، فى ذلك الوقت، فليس فى هذا الكلام إلا تشنيع محض
For a human to have knowledge of its non-existence, these impossibilities would follow; and we do not doubt these forms you have presented, for God the Exalted created for us knowledge that these possibles, He did not do them, and we do not claim that these matters are necessary, but they are possible, it is permissible that they occur and permissible that they do not occur, and the continuity of habit with them, time after time, firmly establishes in our minds their running according to past habit, an establishment from which it does not separate. Rather, it is permissible that a prophet among the prophets - peace be upon them - knows through the ways that he mentioned that so-and-so will not travel from his journey tomorrow, and his arrival is possible, but he knows the non-occurrence of that possible; just as one looks at an ignorant person and knows that he does not know the unseen in any matter, nor does he apprehend intelligibles without teaching, and yet he does not deny that his soul and intuition may become strong such that he apprehends what the prophets apprehend - may God's prayers and peace be upon them - according to what they acknowledge as possible, but they know that that possible did not occur. For if God breaks the habit by bringing it about in a time in which habits are broken, these knowledges would slip from hearts and He would not create them. So there is no barrier to a thing being possible, within the power of God the Exalted, and it having proceeded in His prior knowledge that He will not do it despite its possibility, at certain times, and He creates for us knowledge that He will not do it at that time. There is nothing in this speech except pure objection.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is an argument defending the possibility of prophetic knowledge of future contingents against the philosophers' objections. Ghazali begins by stating that if a human had knowledge of the non-existence of something, certain impossibilities would follow, but he asserts that they do not doubt the scenarios presented. He explains that God created knowledge that certain possibles will not occur, but they remain possible. The continuity of habit reinforces the expectation that the usual pattern will continue. Then he moves to prophetic knowledge: a prophet can know that a possible event will not occur, just as one knows that an ignorant person does not know the unseen. The prophet's knowledge does not negate possibility. If God breaks the habit, the knowledge of non-occurrence would be removed. Thus, there is no contradiction between divine power and prophetic knowledge.

Key Terms
علم
ممكن
عادة
مقدورات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some philosophers argued that if a prophet knows an event will not occur, then that event becomes impossible, thereby limiting God's power.
Correction: Ghazali shows that knowledge of non-occurrence does not change the ontological status of the event; it remains possible (mumkin) and within divine power (fi maqdurat Allah).

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali argue about the relation between prophetic knowledge and the possibility of events?
A) Prophetic knowledge makes events necessary
B) Prophetic knowledge does not negate the possibility of events
C) Events known by prophets are impossible
D) Prophetic knowledge is always uncertain

Key Takeaway
Prophetic knowledge of a possible event's non-occurrence does not strip it of possibility or restrict divine power, because possibility and knowledge are compatible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever feel that because God knows your future sins, you are helpless to change? How does Ghazali's distinction between knowledge and possibility free you from that despair? What will you do today to act on that freedom?
?
When you think about a bad habit you've repeated for years, do you believe it's impossible to break because it's 'your nature'? This lesson teaches that habit does not negate possibility. What one practical step can you take this week to break a habit you thought was unchangeable, trusting that God can remove your old certainty about it?

God's Power to Alter Attributes: The Second Approach to Miracles
Ghazali presents the second approach allowing miracles by altering attributes of fire or prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali defends the possibility of miracles by conceding natural causality but affirming God's power to alter attributes.

Core Idea
God can preserve a prophet from fire by changing the fire's attribute or the prophet's own attribute.

In this passage, Ghazali responds to philosophers who argue that if fire always burns, miracles like Abraham's survival are impossible. He concedes that fire naturally burns similar objects equally, but argues that God can intervene by altering either the fire's attribute or the prophet's attribute, thus preserving the miracle within the framework of cause and effect.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concession
Notice that Ghazali begins by conceding the natural property of fire: it burns similar things equally. This is important because he is not denying causality entirely.
2
Examine the two divine interventions
Ghazali presents two ways God can save a prophet: changing the fire's attribute (confining its heat) or changing the prophet's attribute (giving him resistance). Identify each option in the text.
3
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider how this approach preserves both natural regularity and God's power to create miracles. It shows that miracles need not violate causality but can operate through hidden divine causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الثاني، وفيه الخلاص من هذه التشنيعات، وهو أنا نسلم، أن النار خلقت خلقة، إذا لاقاها، قطنتان متماثلتان، أحرقتهما، ولم تفرق بينهما، إذا كل وجه، ولكن مع هذا نجوز، أن يلقي نبي في النار، فلا يحترق من إما بتغيير صفة النار، أو بتغيير صفة النبي عليه السلام، فيحدث من الله تعالى الملائكة، صفة من النار، تقصر سخونتها على جسمها، بحيث لا تتعداه، من فتبقى معها سخونتها، وتكون على صورة النار وحقيقتها، ولكن لا تتعدى سخونتها وأثرها . أو يحدث فى بدن النبي صفة، لا تخرجه عن كونه لحماً وعظماً، فيدفع أثر النار .
The second approach, and in it deliverance from these objections, namely that we concede that fire was created with a constitution such that if two similar cotton balls encounter it, it would burn them, and it does not differentiate between them in any way, but despite this we allow that a prophet be thrown into the fire and not burn, either by changing the attribute of the fire, or by changing the attribute of the prophet - peace be upon him - so that God the Exalted creates in the angels an attribute of the fire that confines its heat to its body such that it does not exceed it, so its heat remains with it, and it is in the form and reality of fire, but its heat and effect do not exceed. Or He creates in the prophet's body an attribute that does not remove him from being flesh and bone, and it repels the effect of the fire.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage presents the second approach to miracles: conceding the philosophers' premise about fire's nature but showing that God can prevent burning by altering the fire's attribute or the prophet's body. Ghazali describes that fire normally burns similar objects equally, but God can create an attribute in the fire that confines its heat, so the fire remains fire but its heat does not extend, or create an attribute in the prophet's body that repels heat. Thus, the miracle is explained within the framework of causes.

Key Terms
مسلك
تغيير صفة
نار
احتراق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles require the suspension of natural laws entirely, contradicting observed causality.
Correction: Ghazali shows that miracles can occur by God altering attributes while the underlying nature of the fire remains intact, thus preserving causality at one level.

Checkpoint
What are the two ways Ghazali suggests a prophet could be saved from fire according to the second approach?
A) Changing the fire's substance or the prophet's substance
B) Changing the fire's attribute or the prophet's attribute
C) Destroying the fire or the prophet
D) Using a shield or a spell

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's second approach shows that miracles can be explained by God altering attributes of created things, preserving both natural order and divine sovereignty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Ghazali concedes the natural law but still affirms miracles. In your heart, when you experience suffering, do you ever doubt that God could have prevented it by altering attributes? Does your reaction today show trust in His absolute power?

Fire Not Burning a Prophet: Ghazali's Second Approach
Ghazali explains the second approach: fire not burning a prophet through added qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's second approach to miracles, which allows a prophet to be protected from fire without denying fire's causal nature.

Core Idea
Miracles do not deny nature; they suspend its effect by an additional quality.

Ghazali now presents a more nuanced defense of miracles. He concedes that fire by its nature would normally burn two similar pieces of cotton equally. However, he argues that a miracle can occur when God creates an additional quality—either in the fire or in the prophet—that prevents the burning effect without changing the fire's essential nature. This approach preserves both the law of nature and the possibility of miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to how Ghazali structures his argument: concession, then allowance, then explanation of two ways, then analogy, then conclusion.
Notice that Ghazali begins by fully affirming the natural property of fire.
2
Identify the two ways
Find the two ways a prophet can be saved from fire: 1) changing fire's quality so its heat does not extend, 2) changing the prophet's body so it repels the fire's effect. Consider how each preserves the nature of fire.
3
Analyze the talc analogy
Read the analogy of the person who smears himself with talc and enters an oven. Understand how this analogy shows that prevention of burning is empirically possible and not a denial of nature.
The talc does not nullify the fire's heat but provides protection.
4
Reflect on the broader principle
Consider Ghazali's concluding point about the wonders in Allah's power. Ask yourself: why does Ghazali caution against denying possibility? How does this relate to the overall argument against the philosophers?
This connects to the lesson's learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الثاني، وفيه الخلاص من هذه التشنيعات ، وهو أنا نسلم، أن النار خلقت خلقة ، إذا لاقاها ، قطنتان متماثلتان ، أحرقتهما ، ولم تفرق بينهما ، إذا كل وجه ، ولكن مع هذا نجوز ، أن يلقي نبي في النار ، فلا يحترق من إما بتغيير صفة النار ، أو بتغيير صفة النبي عليه السلام ، فيحدث من الله تعالى الملائكة ، صفة من النار ، تقصر سخونتها على جسمها، بحيث لا تتعداه ، من فتبقى معها سخونتها ، وتكون على صورة النار وحقيقتها ، ولكن لا تتعدى سخونتها وأثرها . أو يحدث فى بدن النبي صفة ، لا تخرجه عن كونه لحماً وعظماً، فيدفع أثر النار . فإنا نرى من يطلى نفسه بالطلق (١) ، ثم يعقد في تنور موقد ، ولا يتأثر بالنار ، والذي لم يشاهد ذلك ينكره . فإنكار الخصم اشتمال القدرة ، على إثبات صفة من الصفات ، في النار أو في البدن تمنع من الاحتراق ، كإنكار من لم يشاهد الطلق وأثره ، وفى مقدورات الله تعالى غرائب وعجائب ، ونحن لم نشاهد جميعها فلم ينبغى أن ننكر إمكانها ، ونحكم باستحالتها ؟!
The second approach, in which there is deliverance from these repulsive statements: that we concede that fire was created with a nature such that if two similar pieces of cotton fall upon it, it burns both and does not differentiate between them, in any respect, but along with this we allow that a prophet may be cast into fire and not burn, either by changing the quality of the fire or by changing the quality of the prophet (peace be upon him). So Allah the Exalted creates in the angels a quality in the fire that confines its heat to its own body, so that it does not extend beyond it; thus its heat remains with it, and it retains the form and reality of fire, but its heat and effect do not extend. Or He creates in the body of the prophet a quality that does not remove it from being flesh and bone, and it repels the effect of fire. For we see one who smears himself with talc, then sits in a kindled oven and is not affected by the fire; and one who has not witnessed that denies it. So the opponent's denial that power extends to establishing a quality in the fire or in the body that prevents burning is like the denial of one who has not witnessed the talc and its effect. And in the powers of Allah the Exalted there are wonders and marvels, and we have not witnessed all of them, so why should we deny their possibility and judge them impossible?
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage presents the second approach to refuting the objection against miracles. Ghazali acknowledges that fire by its nature burns two similar pieces of cotton equally, but he allows that a prophet can be thrown into fire and not burn. This is not a denial of fire's nature but a change in either the fire's quality (so its heat does not spread) or the prophet's body (so it repels the heat). He then gives an analogy: a person who applies talc (a protective coating) to his body can enter a hot oven without being burned. Those unfamiliar with this phenomenon might deny it, but experience confirms it. Similarly, the opponent's denial that God can create a quality preventing burning is like the denial of an unexperienced person. Ghazali concludes that since God's power includes many unseen wonders, we should not hastily deny the possibility of such miracles.

طلق
قدرة
إمكان

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali denies the causal power of fire altogether.
Correction: Ghazali affirms that fire by nature burns, but he argues that a miracle can suspend the effect through an added quality without destroying the nature.

Checkpoint

A) A person covered with talc enters a hot oven without being burned.
B) A piece of cotton does not burn when touched by fire.
C) The prophet's body turns into a different substance.
D) The fire loses all its heat.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's second approach shows that miracles are possible without denying the inherent causal properties of created things.

?

Accelerating Natural Transformations as a Miraculous Proof
Ghazali argues for miracles by accelerating natural transformations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the concept of acceleration of natural processes to argue for the possibility of miracles like reviving the dead and turning a staff into a serpent.

Core Idea
If matter can change slowly, God can make it change instantly.

Ghazali continues his defense of miracles by showing that the same principles that apply to turning a staff into a serpent also apply to reviving the dead. He uses the natural cycle of transformation from earth to plant to blood to semen to animal as an example of matter's inherent changeability. The key move is that God can accelerate these transformations, so the opponent cannot deny miracles on the basis of natural timescales.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Notice the flow from general statement to specific examples to rhetorical question.
The phrase 'له، دماً' might be a scribal spacing; it means 'to it, blood'.
2
Trace the natural cycle
Identify each transformation: earth to plant, plant to blood, blood to semen, semen to animal. This is the normal process.
This cycle is key to Ghazali's argument.
3
Understand the acceleration argument
Ghazali asks why God cannot speed up this cycle. Since there is no lower limit, it can happen instantly. That is the miracle.
The opponent must accept this if they accept the natural cycle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك إحياء الميت ، وقلب العصا حية، يمكن بهذه الطريق، وهو أن المادة قابلة لكل شيء، فالتراب وسائر العناصر، يستحيل نباتاً، ثم النبات يستحيل، عند أكل الحيوان، له، دماً، ثم الدم يستحيل منيا، ثم المنى ينصب، فيتخلق حيواناً، وهذا بحكم العادة واقع في زمان متطاول، فلم يحيل الخصم أن يكون في مقدورات الله تعالى، أن يدير المادة في هذه الأطوار، في وقت أقرب مما عهد فيه ؟! وإذا جاز فى وقت أقرب، فلا ضبط للأقل فيستعجل هذه القوى فى عملها، ويحصل به ما هو معجزة للنبي عليه السلام .
Similarly, reviving the dead and turning the staff into a serpent are possible by this method: that matter is receptive to everything. So earth and the other elements become plants, then plants when eaten by an animal become blood, then blood becomes semen, then semen is emitted and an animal is formed. This by custom occurs over a long time. So why would the opponent deem it impossible that it is within the powers of Allah the Exalted to turn matter through these stages in a shorter time than what is customary? And if it is permissible in a shorter time, there is no lower limit to the shortest. So He accelerates these powers in their work, and thereby what is a miracle for the prophet (peace be upon him) occurs.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage extends the same reasoning to other miracles. Ghazali explains that matter (mādda) is inherently changeable, as seen in the natural cycle from earth to plants to blood to semen to animal. This transformation normally takes a long time, but God can accelerate it. Since acceleration is possible, the opponent cannot deny that God could cause a dead person to revive or a staff to become a serpent in an instant. The argument rests on the plasticity of matter and the relativity of time.

Key Terms
إحياء
مادة
استحالة
معجزة
تعجيل
قوى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali denies that natural transformations take time.
Correction: He affirms the natural cycle but argues God can accelerate it.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for Ghazali's argument that reviving the dead is possible?
A) The dead can be brought back by angels directly.
B) Matter transforms over time, so God can accelerate the process.
C) The prophet has inherent power over life and death.
D) The laws of nature are not fixed and can be violated.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that since matter naturally changes over time, God can accelerate those changes to produce miracles instantly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Ghazali argues that God can accelerate any natural process. Which area of your life do you need to ask Him to accelerate spiritual growth, instead of waiting for it to happen 'naturally'?
?
You believe in miracles, but do you truly believe God can change you instantly? Or do you, like the opponent, limit His power by assuming the slow pace of nature is the only way? What would it take for you to ask God for an immediate transformation today?

The Source of Miraculous Power: Objection and Answer
Ghazali answers whether miraculous power comes from the prophet's soul or another principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that miraculous power does not originate from the prophet's soul independently but ultimately comes from Allah, with the prophet's aspiration serving as a condition for its manifestation.

Core Idea
Miracles are from Allah, not the prophet's soul; the prophet's desire is a condition, not a cause.

Ghazali here confronts a key objection: does the miraculous power come from the prophet's soul or from some other principle? He turns the question back on the opponent and then provides a positive account that attributes all miraculous events to Allah, while explaining the role of the prophet's aspiration (himma) and the system of good in their occurrence. This passage is crucial for understanding the Islamic perspective on miracles and the relationship between divine action and human agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the full translation. Pay attention to the structure: an objection is raised, then Ghazali responds.
2
Identify the objection and Ghazali's counter-question
The objection asks whether miraculous power comes from the prophet's soul or another principle. Ghazali responds with a counter-question, turning the opponent's own concession against them.
Notice how Ghazali uses the opponent's admission about natural phenomena.
3
Understand Ghazali's positive doctrine
Ghazali states that the best course for both parties is to attribute all miraculous events to Allah, either directly or through angels. This is the core of his answer.
This establishes divine agency as the ultimate source.
4
Examine the role of himma (aspiration) and the system of good
Ghazali explains that the prophet's aspiration (himma) turns toward the miracle when its occurrence is needed for the perpetuation of the Law. The system of good determines its manifestation, and the generous principle emanates only when need becomes preponderant.
The prophet's role is conditional, not causal.
5
Reflect on the closing argument about transmitted miracles
Ghazali argues that if the opponent accepts any special characteristic for the prophet that contravenes custom, they must also accept miracles reported by multiple chains of transmission. Denying them would be inconsistent.
This ties the discussion to the broader issue of accepting revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : وهذا يصدر من نفس النبي ؟! أو من مبدأ آخر من المبادئ عند اقتراح النبي عليه السلام ؟! قلنا : وما سلمتموه من جواز نزول الأمطار ، والصواعق ، وتزلزل الأرض بقوة نفس النبي ، يحصل منه أو من مبدأ آخر ؟! فقولنا في هذا كقولكم في ذاك ، والأولى بنا وبكم إضافة ذلك ، إلى الله تعالى ، إما بغير واسطة ، أو بواسطة الملائكة ، لكن وقت استحقاق حصولها ، انصرفت همة النبي عليه السلام ، إليها ، ويعين نظام الخير فى ظهورها لاستمرار نظام الشرع ، فيكون ذلك مرجحاً جهة الوجود ، ويكون الشيء فى نفسه ممكناً ، والمبدأ به سمحاً جواداً ، ولكن لا يفيض منه إلا إذا ترجحت الحاجة إلى وجوده ، وصار الخير متعيناً فيه ، ولا يصير الخير متعيناً فيه ، إلا إذا احتاج نبي ، في إثبات نبوته إليه ، لإفاضة الخير . فهذا كله لائق بمساق كلامهم ، ولازم لهم ، مهما فتحوا باب الاختصاص للنبي عليه السلام . بخاصة تخالف عادة الناس ، فإن مقادير ذلك الاختصاص يجب معه التكذيب ، لما تواتر نقله وورد لا ينضبط في العقل إمكانه ، الشرع بتصديقه . فلم يجب
If it is said: 'And does this proceed from the prophet's soul? Or from another principle among the principles at the prophet's request (peace be upon him)?' We say: 'And what you have conceded regarding the permissibility of sending down rain, thunderbolts, and causing earthquakes by the power of the prophet's soul — does that proceed from him or from another principle? So our statement in this is like your statement in that. And the better course for us and you is to attribute all that to Allah the Exalted, either without intermediary or through the angels. But at the time when it becomes worthy of occurrence, the prophet's aspiration (himma) turns toward it, and the system of good determines its manifestation for the perpetuation of the system of the Law. So that becomes a preponderating factor for the aspect of existence. And the thing is in itself possible, and the principle is generous and bountiful, but it only emanates when the need for its existence becomes preponderant and the good becomes determined in it. And the good does not become determined in it except when a prophet needs it to establish his prophethood for the diffusion of good. All this is consistent with the course of their speech, and a necessary consequence for them, whenever they open the door of specialization for the prophet (peace be upon him) with a special characteristic that contravenes the custom of people. For the measures of that specialization must entail disbelief [in what is transmitted] because what is transmitted by many and received by the Law as true — its possibility cannot be limited by the intellect. So it is not necessary (to deny it).'
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection: does the miraculous power originate from the prophet's soul (nafs) or from another principle? Ghazali responds by turning the question back to the opponent, who already admits that natural phenomena like rain and earthquakes can occur via the prophet's soul. The better approach for both parties is to attribute all such events to Allah, either directly or through angels. The prophet's aspiration (himma) serves as a condition for the emanation from the generous principle, but only when needed for establishing prophethood. Ghazali then argues that if the opponent accepts any special characteristic for the prophet that contravenes custom, they must also accept transmitted miracles, otherwise they would be denying what is established by multiple chains of transmission and confirmed by the Law. Such denial is not necessary.
