General Argument on Miracles Through Emanation and Generation
Ghazali argues from emanation theory and spontaneous generation to open a door for miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the philosophers' own theory of emanation and observation of spontaneous generation to argue for the possibility of miracles, even though the argument is incomplete.

Core Idea
Nature's hidden variables leave room for miracles even within emanation theory.

Ghazali continues his defense of miracles by turning the philosophers' own system against them. He points to examples like spontaneous generation, which the philosophers themselves accept, to show that the factors determining which form matter receives are not fully known. This opens a door for miracles, which could occur through unknown preparedness, even within the emanation framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Note the flow of argument from the typical generation of animals to spontaneous generation.
The passage is cut off at the end; try to infer the missing conclusion.
2
Identify the philosophers' principle
The philosophers believe forms flow from angels based on the preparedness of matter. The sperm of a horse predisposes it to receive the horse form. This explains regular generation.
3
Consider spontaneous generation
Ghazali points out that some animals (worms, mice) are generated from dust without a parent. Their forms still come from angels, but the factors determining their preparedness are unknown to humans.
This shows that our knowledge of natural regularities is incomplete.
4
Connect to miracles
Since humans do not know all possible degrees of preparedness, they cannot claim a miracle (like a staff becoming a snake) is impossible. The angelic emanation could produce that form if the matter is prepared.
This is an argument from the philosophers' own premises to allow miracles.
5
Reflect on the missing conclusion
The argument is cut off, but likely Ghazali concludes that miracles are possible within emanation theory. Think about how the argument would complete.
Consider what would happen if the prophets bring about such preparedness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى الجملة ، لما كان لا يقبل صورة الحيوان ، إلا النطفة ، وإنما تفيض القوى الحيوانية عليها من الملائكة ، التي هي مبادئ الموجودات عندهم ، ولم يتخلق قط من نطفة الإنسان إلا إنسان ، ومن نطفة الفرس إلا فرس ، من حيث إن حصوله من الفرس ، أوجب ترجيحاً ، لمناسبة صورة الفرس ، على سائر الصور ، فلم يقبل إلا الصورة المترجحة بهذا الطريق ، لذلك لم ينبت قط من الشعير حنطة ولا من بذر الكمثرى ، تفاح ، ثم رأينا أجناساً من الحيوانات ، تتولد من التراب ، لا تتوالد قط كالديدان، ومنها ما يتولد ويتوالد جميعاً ، كالفأرة والحية ، والعقرب ، وكان تولدها من التراب ، ويختلف استعدادها لقبول الصور ، بأمور غابت عنا ، ولم يكن فى القوة البشرية الاطلاع عليها ، إذ ليس تفيض الصور عندهم، من الملائكة بالتشهى ولا جزافاً ، بل لا يفيض
In summary, since the animal form is only received by the sperm, and the animal powers flow upon it from the angels, which are the principles of existents according to them, and no animal has ever been generated from a human sperm except a human, nor from a horse's sperm except a horse, because the fact that it came from the horse gave preponderance to the suitability of the horse's form over other forms, so it did not accept except the form preponderated by this path. For this reason wheat has never grown from barley nor apples from a pear seed. Then we have seen species of animals that are generated from dust, not generated at all, like worms, and some that are both generated from dust and reproduce, like the mouse, snake, and scorpion. Their generation from dust occurs, and their preparedness to accept forms differs due to things hidden from us. And it is not within human capacity to know them, because according to them forms do not flow from the angels by desire or arbitrarily, but rather they do not flow...
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage presents a further argument based on the philosophers' own theory of emanation (fayḍ). It notes that animal forms typically come from specific seeds (sperm) and produce their own kind, but there are also animals generated spontaneously from dust (such as worms, mice, etc.). This shows that matter has different degrees of preparedness (isti'dād) for receiving forms, and the precise factors are unknown to humans. Therefore, the opponent cannot invoke fixed natural laws to deny miracles like a staff turning into a snake, because the forms that flow from the angels are not haphazard but depend on preparedness. Since humans do not know all varieties of preparedness, they cannot rule out the possibility of a form occurring out of its usual context as a miracle. The argument is cut off at the end of the page.

Diverse Preparations in Nature and Their Implications
Ghazali argues from natural examples that preparations are diverse and not fully known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses examples from natural generation and talismans to show that preparations (istidad) for forms are diverse and hidden, thereby leaving room for miracles.

Core Idea
Nature's own diversity reveals that preparations are not limited to fixed patterns, allowing for the extraordinary.

Ghazali continues his critique of the philosophers' causal determinism by turning to nature itself. He points out that while some generation is regular (like from sperm), other generation is spontaneous and mysterious (like worms from dust). Moreover, the art of talismans shows that human knowledge can combine celestial and mineral powers to produce strange effects. This diversity in preparation suggests that we cannot claim to know all possible causes, thus leaving the door open for miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the flow from regular generation to spontaneous generation to talismans.
Pay attention to the key terms: استعداد (preparedness), طلسمات (talismans).
2
Identify the three examples used
The passage presents three sets of examples: (1) sperm generating only its kind, (2) animals generated from dust (spontaneous generation), and (3) talismans producing effects. List each and note how they show different kinds of preparation.
The examples are cumulative; each adds a layer of hiddenness.
3
Understand the argument's structure
Ghazali first states the fixed pattern (sperm to like kind), then introduces exceptions (spontaneous generation) to show that preparations can be hidden, then elevates to talismans which actively manipulate celestial-mineral mixtures. This implies that the principles of preparation are vast and not fully known.
4
Reflect on the implication
If preparations are so diverse and partially beyond human knowledge, then one cannot deny that a new preparation could arise—like a prophet raising the dead. This supports the possibility of miracles.
This connects to Ghazali's broader argument against the philosophers' necessary causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى الجملة ، لما كان لا يقبل صورة الحيوان ، إلا النطفة ، وإنما تفيض القوى الحيوانية عليها من الملائكة ، التي هي مبادئ الموجودات عندهم ، ولم يتخلق قط من نطفة الإنسان إلا إنسان ، ومن نطفة الفرس إلا فرس ، من حيث إن حصوله من الفرس ، أوجب ترجيحاً ، لمناسبة صورة الفرس ، على سائر الصور ، فلم يقبل إلا الصورة المترجحة بهذا الطريق ، لذلك لم ينبت قط من الشعير حنطة ولا من بذر الكمثرى ، تفاح ، ثم رأينا أجناساً من الحيوانات ، تتولد من التراب ، لا تتوالد قط كالديدان، ومنها ما يتولد ويتوالد جميعاً ، كالفأرة والحية ، والعقرب ، وكان تولدها من التراب ، ويختلف استعدادها لقبول الصور ، بأمور غابت عنا ، ولم يكن فى القوة البشرية الاطلاع عليها ، إذ ليس تفيض الصور عندهم، من الملائكة بالتشهى ولا جزافاً ، بل لا يفيض على كل محل إلا ما تعين قبوله له ، لكونه مستعداً في نفسه ، والاستعدادات مختلفة ، ومبادئها عندهم امتزاجات الكواكب، واختلاف نسب الأجرام العلوية في حركاتها . فقد اتضح من هذا ، أن مبادئ الاستعدادات ، فيها غرائب وعجائب ، حتى توصل أرباب الطلسمات ، من علم خواص الجواهر المعدنية وعلم النجوم ، إلى مزج القوى السماوية ، بالخواص المعدنية بالخواص المعدنية ، فاتخذوا أشكالاً الأرضية ، وطلبوا لها طالعاً مخصوصاً من الطوالع ، وأحدثوا بها أموراً غريبة في العالم، فربما دفعوا الحية العقرب ، عن بلد ، والبق عن بلد ، إلى غير ذلك من أمور ، تعرف من علم الطلسمات
In summary: because the sperm accepts only the form of an animal, and the animal powers flow onto it from the angels, which are the principles of existing things in their view. And never from human sperm was generated anything but a human, nor from a horse's sperm anything but a horse, because its being from the horse necessitates a preponderance for the appropriateness of the form of the horse over all other forms, so it accepts only the preponderant form by this path. For this reason, barley never grew into wheat, nor did pear seed produce apples. Then we see species of animals that are generated from dust and never reproduce, like worms; and some that are both generated and reproduce, like the mouse, the snake, and the scorpion. Their generation is from dust. And their preparedness (istidad) to accept forms differs due to matters hidden from us, and it is not within human power to discover them. For, according to them, forms do not flow from the angels capriciously or haphazardly; rather, nothing flows onto any substrate except what it is determined to accept, because it is prepared in itself. Preparations are diverse, and their principles in their view are the mixtures of planets and the varying proportions of the celestial bodies in their motions. From this it is clear that the principles of preparations contain wonders and marvels, to the extent that the masters of talismans, from the science of the properties of minerals and astrology, have succeeded in mixing celestial powers with mineral properties, so they take earthly forms and seek for them a specific ascendant among the ascendants, and thereby produce strange things in the world. Perhaps they repel the snake and scorpion from a land, or lice from a land, and other such things known from the science of talismans.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is an analogical argument using natural generation and talismans. Al-Ghazali begins by stating that animal forms come only through sperm, and that from human sperm only a human comes, etc. This seems to show fixed patterns. But then he points out that there are also animals that generate spontaneously from dust, like worms, and others that both generate and reproduce, like mice. Their preparedness (istidad) to accept forms varies due to hidden causes. Furthermore, the principles of these preparations are, in the philosophers' view, the mixtures of planets and the motions of celestial bodies. Then he notes that the masters of talismans (arbab al-tilismat) actually combine celestial forces with mineral properties to produce effects like repelling snakes or lice. This shows that the principles of preparations are full of wonders and are not fully known. The implied conclusion is that since we cannot fully grasp all preparations, we cannot deny that a body might suddenly acquire a new preparation leading to a miracle.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That fixed patterns in nature (e.g., seeds producing like kind) prove that all events are determined by necessary causation, leaving no room for miracles.
Correction: Ghazali shows that nature itself contains diverse generation patterns, including spontaneous generation and talismanic effects, indicating that preparations are varied and hidden. Therefore, one cannot use observed patterns to deny the possibility of miracles.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness a fixed pattern in nature—like a child resembling its parents—do you ever assume that Allah cannot break that pattern? Have you ever doubted a miracle in your heart simply because you think you 'know' how the world works?
?
Ghazali says that the principles of preparations contain wonders and marvels beyond human discovery. Does this humble you? Are you willing to admit that you do not and cannot know all that Allah can do—or do you still secretly rely on your own limited understanding?
?
The masters of talismans learned to combine earthly and heavenly forces to produce strange effects. How much more powerful is the One who created both? Do you truly trust that Allah can bring about what seems impossible to you, or do you still hold back, waiting for a 'natural' explanation before you believe?

The Root of Denying Miracles: Narrow-Mindedness
Ghazali defends miracles by attributing their denial to narrow-mindedness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that rejecting miracles reflects limited understanding of God's creative mysteries, not rational impossibility.

Core Idea
Denying miracles is not logic—it's narrow-mindedness.

Ghazali challenges the philosophers' rejection of miracles by pointing to the very principles of nature they rely on. He argues that if we cannot fully comprehend the ordinary preparations in the world, we cannot rule out miraculous transformations. In this passage, he uses the example of talismans to show how little we understand of nature's secrets, and then concludes that denial of miracles stems from intellectual complacency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the full argument. Notice how Ghazali moves from general principles to a specific example.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key claim about preparations
Ghazali states that preparations (istiʿdādāt) are diverse and their principles involve celestial mixtures. This sets up the idea that nature is complex and not fully understood.
The philosophers themselves admit this.
3
Examine the talisman example
Ghazali describes how talisman masters use knowledge of mineral properties and astrology to produce strange effects. Ask yourself: What does this example show about human knowledge?
It shows the limits of our control over natural processes.
4
Trace the argument to miracles
Ghazali then applies the same reasoning: if we cannot fully grasp natural preparations, how can we claim that a miraculous transformation is impossible? Notice the rhetorical question.
The key phrase: 'how can we know the impossibility...?'
5
Reflect on the diagnosis
Ghazali concludes that denial of miracles is due to narrow-mindedness (ḍīq al-ḥawṣala) and heedlessness of God's secrets. Think about why he considers this a fault.
He contrasts this with the open-mindedness of those who study the wonders of science.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على كل محل إلا ما تعين قبوله له ، لكونه مستعداً في نفسه ، والاستعدادات مختلفة ، ومبادئها عندهم امتزاجات الكواكب، واختلاف نسب الأجرام العلوية في حركاتها . فقد اتضح من هذا ، أن مبادئ الاستعدادات ، فيها غرائب وعجائب ، حتى توصل أرباب الطلسمات ، من علم خواص الجواهر المعدنية وعلم النجوم ، إلى مزج القوى السماوية ، بالخواص المعدنية فاتخذوا أشكالاً أرضية ، وطلبوا لها طالعاً مخصوصاً من الطوالع ، وأحدثوا بها أموراً غريبة في العالم، فربما دفعوا الحية والعقرب عن بلد ، والبق عن بلد ، إلى غير ذلك من أمور تعرف من علم الطلسمات . فإذا خرجت عن الضبط مبادئ الاستعدادات ، ولم نقف على كنهها ولم يكن لنا سبيل إلى حصرها ، فمن أين نعلم استحالة حصول الاستعداد في بعض الأجسام للاستحالة في الأطوار في أقرب زمان حتى يستعد لقبول صورة ما كان يستعد لها من قبل وينهض ذلك معجزة . ما إنكار هذا إلا لضيق الحوصلة ، والأنس بالموجودات العالية ، والذهول عن أسرار الله سبحانه في الخلقة والفطرة . ومن استقرأ عجائب العلوم لم يستبعد من قدرة الله تعالى ما يحكى من معجزات الأنبياء عليهم السلام بحال من الأحوال
Every locus except what is determined for acceptance [is possible], due to its being prepared in itself. Preparations are diverse, and their principles according to them are mixtures of planets and the differing relations of celestial bodies in their movements. It has become clear from this that the principles of preparations contain wonders and marvels, such that the masters of talismans, from knowledge of the properties of mineral gems and astrology, arrived at mixing celestial powers with mineral properties. They took earthly forms, sought for them a particular ascendant among the ascendants, and produced thereby strange things in the world—perhaps they drove away the snake and scorpion from a country, and the bedbug from a country, and other things known from the science of talismans. Since the principles of preparations escape control, and we do not comprehend their reality, nor have we a way to encompass them, how can we know the impossibility of the occurrence of a preparation in some bodies for transformation in states in the shortest time so that it becomes prepared to receive a form for which it was not previously prepared, and that becomes a miracle? Denial of this is nothing but narrowness of capacity, familiarity with ordinary existents, and heedlessness of God's secrets in creation and nature. Whoever contemplates the wonders of sciences will not deem improbable, in any way, what is narrated of the miracles of the prophets, peace be upon them, from the power of God Almighty.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is an argument for the possibility of miracles. Ghazali begins with the empirical observation that preparations (istiʿdādāt) for receiving forms in matter are diverse and mysterious; their principles, according to the philosophers, involve planetary mixtures and varying celestial motions. He notes that the practitioners of talismans, leveraging their knowledge of mineral properties and astrology, have produced astonishing effects—e.g., repelling snakes or bugs from a region. This shows that the principles of preparation are beyond human control and comprehension. If we cannot fully grasp these natural preparations, how can we confidently assert that a preparation for a miraculous transformation (such as a staff becoming a snake) is impossible? Ghazali then diagnoses the root of such denial: narrow-mindedness (ḍīq al-ḥawṣala), being too accustomed to ordinary phenomena, and forgetting God's mysteries in creation. He concludes that anyone who studies the wonders of sciences will not find miracles improbable, since they are within God's power. The argument is inductive: since lesser wonders (talismans) occur, greater wonders (prophetic miracles) are not to be rejected.

Key Terms

طلسمات

ضيق الحوصلة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles are rationally impossible because natural laws are fixed and unchangeable.
Correction: Ghazali shows that natural principles are not fully known or controllable; thus we cannot rule out miraculous transformations. Denial stems from narrow-mindedness, not necessity.

Checkpoint

C) Lack of scientific evidence

Key Takeaway

God Moving a Dead Hand: Refuting Objections on Possibility and Distinction of Movement
Ghazali defends possibility of God moving a dead hand and distinguishes voluntary from involuntary movement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali considers God moving a dead hand to produce ordered writing as possible in itself, and how he distinguishes voluntary from involuntary movement through inner awareness rather than external criteria.

Core Idea
Miracles are not impossible, they just break the habit we observe.

In this passage from the Tahafut, Ghazali defends the possibility of extraordinary acts by God, such as moving a dead hand to write. He faces two objections: that such an act would undermine the link between order and knowledge, and that it would erase the distinction between voluntary and involuntary movement. Ghazali responds by grounding the distinction in our immediate inner experience, not in external observation. This short but dense argument reveals a key principle of Ash'ari occasionalism: the world runs on habit, not necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without worrying about details. Then read the translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the contrast between 'mustahīl' (impossible) and 'mustankar li-ṭirād al-ʿāda' (rejected due to habit).
2
Identify the two objections
Ghazali presents and refutes two objections. Find them: one about the indication of knowledge, and one about the tremor vs. voluntary movement distinction.
The objections begin with 'wa qawlukum...'.
3
Understand Ghazali's replies
For the first objection, Ghazali says the agent is God who is all-knowing. For the second, he appeals to a necessary inner distinction we feel between voluntary and involuntary movement.
The term 'qudra' (power) expresses that inner difference.
4
Connect to the broader theme
Reflect on how this passage illustrates the Ash'ari view that habits are not necessary laws, and that our knowledge of the world is based on God's custom, not logical necessity.
This ties into the debate on causality with the philosophers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تحريك الله تعالى يد الميت ، ونصبه على صورة حي يقعد ، ويكتب حتى تحدث من حركة يده الكتابة المنظومة ، فليس بمستحيل في نفسه ، مهما أحلنا الحوادث إلى إرادة مختار ، وإنما هو مستنكر لإطراد العادة بخلافه وقولكم تبطل به دلالة إحكام الفعل ، على علم الفاعل ، فليس كذلك ، فإن الفاعل الآن هو الله تعالى ، وهو المحكم ، وهو عالم به . وأما قولكم : إنه لا يبقى فرق بين الرعشة والحركة المختارة ، فنقول : إنما أدركنا ذلك من أنفسنا، لأنا شاهدنا من أنفسنا تفرقة ضرورية، بين الحالين ، فعبرنا عن ذلك الفرق بالقدرة ، فعرفنا أن الواقع من القسمين الممكنين ، أحدهما في حال ، والآخر في حال ، وهو إيجاد الحركة مع القدرة عليها في حال ، وإيجاد الحركة دون القدرة فى حال أخرى . وأما إذا نظرنا إلى غيرنا ، ورأينا حركات كثيرة منظومة ، حصل لنا بقدرته ، فهذه علوم يخلقها الله تعالى بمجارى العادات ، نعرف بها وجود أحد قسمى الإمكان ، ولا نبين به استحالة القسم الثاني ؛ كما سبق
As for God Most High moving the hand of a dead person, and setting him up in the form of a living person who sits and writes, so that from the movement of his hand ordered writing occurs, that is not impossible in itself, as long as we refer events to a freely choosing will. Rather, it is rejected because of the contrary continuous habit. And your saying that it invalidates the indication of the perfection of the act upon the knowledge of the agent is not so. For the agent now is God Most High, and He is the one who perfects, and He is knowing of it. As for your saying that no distinction remains between tremor and voluntary movement, we say: we have only perceived that from ourselves, because we have witnessed within ourselves a necessary distinction between the two states. So we expressed that distinction by the term 'power' (qudra). Thus we know that what occurs of the two possible kinds is one in one state and the other in another state: the bringing about of movement together with power over it in one state, and the bringing about of movement without power in another state. As for when we look at others and see many ordered movements, we derive that it is by His power. These are knowledges that God creates by the course of habit, by which we know the existence of one of the two possibilities, but we do not thereby demonstrate the impossibility of the second possibility, as previously.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection regarding God's power to produce ordered movements in a dead body. Ghazali first states that God moving a dead hand to write ordered text is not inherently impossible, as long as one acknowledges that events are referred to a freely choosing will (إرادة مختار). It is only deemed improbable because it contradicts habitual experience, not because it is logically impossible. Then he refutes two objections. The first objection claims that such an act would invalidate the evidence that a perfectly ordered act indicates the agent's knowledge. Ghazali replies that the agent here is God, who is perfectly knowing and perfecting, so the order still indicates knowledge. The second objection says that if God can produce ordered movements in a dead body, there would be no distinction between a tremor (involuntary) and voluntary movement. Ghazali responds that the distinction is known from our own inner experience: we have a necessary, immediate awareness of the difference between a voluntary movement (accompanied by power/qudra) and an involuntary one (without power). We express that inner difference by the term 'power'. So the distinction is real and based on self-perception, not on external observation. Finally, Ghazali notes that when we observe ordered movements in others, we infer they are by God's power, but this is knowledge from habitual course (مجرى العادة); it does not prove the impossibility of the alternative (i.e., that the movement could be without power).

Key Terms
تحريك

إرادة مختار
إطراد العادة
الرعشة
الحركة المختارة
قدرة
علوم ضرورية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the distinction between voluntary and involuntary movement is based on external observable criteria such as order or regularity.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that the distinction is an immediate, necessary inner awareness we have of our own states, which we label as 'power' (qudra); it is not inferred from external observation.

Checkpoint
How does Ghazali say we distinguish between a voluntary movement and a tremor?

B) By a necessary internal distinction we directly perceive in ourselves
C) By the presence of an external agent
D) By the absence of any movement

Key Takeaway
Extraordinary acts like God moving a dead hand are possible because they only break habit, not logical necessity, and our knowledge of voluntary movement comes from immediate inner awareness, not external observation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of your own actions, do you ever pause to sense the inner distinction between what you choose and what simply happens to you? How does that immediate awareness shape your understanding of being accountable before God?
?
If every movement you make—even the ordered writing you produce—is ultimately brought about by God's power, what does that do to your sense of personal ownership and humility? Can you still say 'I did this' without pride?
?
Consider a moment when you witnessed an extraordinary event or a 'coincidence' that seemed to break the normal habit of things. Did you immediately attribute it to God's power, or did you search for natural causes? What would it take for you to trust that God can act directly, even when your mind resists?

Refutation of the Philosopher's Objection on God Moving a Dead Hand
Ghazali refutes the philosopher's objection about God moving a dead hand to write.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali defends the possibility of God moving a dead hand to produce orderly writing by distinguishing between habitual impossibility and logical impossibility, and by grounding the concept of power (qudra) in human self-awareness.

Core Idea
What is merely contrary to habit is not impossible for God; 'power' is a distinction we perceive in ourselves.

This passage addresses a philosophical objection against the occasionalist doctrine that all events are directly caused by God. The philosophers argued that if God could move a dead hand to write, then voluntary actions would be indistinguishable from involuntary ones, and the argument from design would collapse. Ghazali responds by clarifying the concepts of possibility, habit, and the internal perception of power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to understand Ghazali's flow of argument.
Notice that Ghazali answers multiple objections in sequence.
2
Identify the philosophical objection
Identify what the philosophers claimed: that moving a dead hand is impossible and would invalidate the argument from design.
This objection is implied in the passage; Ghazali is responding to it.
3
Understand Ghazali's first two responses
Ghazali says the act is not impossible, only contrary to habit. Also, the act still shows God's knowledge because He is the agent.
4
Analyze the distinction between tremor and voluntary movement
Ghazali argues we perceive a necessary distinction in ourselves, which we call 'power' (qudra). This is a direct experience, not a rational inference.
This grounds the concept of power in human self-awareness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تحريك الله تعالى يد الميت ، ونصبه على صورة حي يقعد ، ويكتب حتى تحدث من حركة يده الكتابة المنظومة ، فليس بمستحيل في نفسه ، مهما أحلنا الحوادث إلى إرادة مختار ، وإنما هو مستنكر لإطراد العادة بخلافه وقولكم تبطل به دلالة إحكام الفعل ، على علم الفاعل ، فليس كذلك ،
فإن الفاعل الآن هو الله تعالى ، وهو المحكم ، وهو عالم بـ[؟] به .
وأما قولكم : إنه لا يبقى فرق بين الرعشة والحركة المختارة ، فنقول : إنما أدركنا ذلك من أنفسنا، لأنا شاهدنا من أنفسنا تفرقة ضرورية، بين الحالين ، فعبرنا عن ذلك الفرق بالقدرة ، فعرفنا أن الواقع من القسمين الممكنين ، أحدهما في حال ، والآخر في حال ، وهو إيجاد الحركة مع القدرة عليها في حال ، وإيجاد الحركة دون القدرة فى حال أخرى .
وأما إذا نظرنا إلى غيرنا ، ورأينا حركات كثيرة منظومة ، حصل لنا بقدرته ، فهذه علوم يخلقها الله تعالى بمجارى العادات ، نعرف بها وجود أحد قسمى الإمكان ، ولا نبين به استحالة القسم الثاني ؛ كما سبق
And as for God moving the hand of a dead person, and setting him up in the form of a living person who sits and writes so that orderly writing occurs from the movement of his hand, that is not impossible in itself, as long as we attribute events to a free will. Rather, it is rejected because it contradicts the normal course of habit. And your statement that it invalidates the indication of the perfection of the act upon the knowledge of the agent is not so. For the agent now is God, and He is the perfecter, and He knows it. And as for your statement that there remains no difference between tremor and voluntary movement, we say: we have perceived that from ourselves, for we have witnessed from ourselves a necessary distinction between the two states, and we expressed that distinction by 'power' (qudra). So we know that of the two possible divisions, one occurs in one state and the other in another state, namely, the bringing into existence of movement with power over it in one state, and the bringing into existence of movement without power in another state. And as for when we look at others and see many orderly movements, we conclude that they are by His power. These are knowledges that God creates according to the habitual course, by which we know the existence of one of the two possibilities, but we do not thereby demonstrate the impossibility of the second possibility, as previously.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a philosopher's objection. The philosophers had claimed that if God could move a dead hand to produce orderly writing, then there would be no difference between a tremor and voluntary movement, and the argument from design (the perfection of the act indicating knowledge of the agent) would collapse. Ghazali responds: (1) Such an act is not impossible in itself, because all events are attributed to a free agent; it is only contrary to habit, so it is not impossible. (2) The perfect act still indicates God's knowledge, because the agent is God Himself. (3) The distinction between voluntary movement and tremor is a necessary distinction that we perceive in ourselves; we call this distinction 'power' (qudra). (4) When we see orderly movements in others, we infer they are by God's power, but that does not prove the opposite is impossible; we only know one possibility exists, not that the other is impossible.

Key Terms
تحريك
قدرة
عادة
إمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates all actions directly, then there is no difference between voluntary and involuntary movements.
Correction: Ghazali argues that we directly perceive a necessary distinction between the two states, which we call 'power' (qudra). This distinction is grounded in human self-awareness, not in the nature of the action itself.

Checkpoint

C) The normal course of habit
D) The intention of the agent

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that the philosopher's objection fails because what is only contrary to habit is not impossible for God, and the distinction between voluntary and involuntary movement is grounded in our direct experience of power.

?
This passage teaches that what contradicts habit is not impossible for Allah. What habits in your life have you come to treat as unchangeable—and what would it mean to truly believe that Allah can interrupt them at any moment? Will you ask Him to do so today?

Ghazali's Refutation of the Indivisible Atom Objection
Ghazali refutes the claim that knowledge resides in an indivisible atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument against the claim that knowledge resides in an indivisible atom, and why this objection fails to save the philosophers' position.

Core Idea
The indivisible atom cannot be the locus of knowledge because power and will are not localized in body parts.

In this passage, Ghazali addresses a potential objection from the philosophers who might try to salvage their theory by positing that knowledge resides in an indivisible atom (jawhar fard). He quickly dismisses this by showing that it leads to further problems, as power and will would also have to be in that same atom, yet we observe that they are not tied to specific body parts. This refutation reinforces the earlier argument that knowledge cannot be localized in a divisible substance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage clearly
Read the Arabic passage once without interpreting. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: an objection followed by a refutation.
The passage is concise and dense.
2
Identify the objector’s claim
The objector suggests that knowledge resides in an indivisible atom (jawhar fard) to avoid problems of division. Identify this claim.
The atom is a hypothetical indivisible substance.
3
Understand Ghazali’s response
Ghazali first says discussing the atom is lengthy and doesn’t solve the problem. Then he argues that power and will would also have to be in that atom, but they are not located in the hand (e.g., knowledge of writing remains after amputation).
Focus on the example of writing.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Conclude that the atom cannot be the locus of all mental faculties, so the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : هلا دفعتم هذه البراهين ، بأن العلم يحل من الجسم في جوهر متحيز لا يتجزأ ، وهو الجوهر الفرد ؟ قلنا : لأن الكلام فى الجوهر الفرد ، يتعلق بأمور هندسية ، يطول القول في حلها ثم ليس فيه ما يدفع الإشكال ، فإنه يلزم أن تكون القدرة والإرادة ، أيضاً فى ذلك الجزء ، فإن للإنسان فعلا ولا يتصور ذلك إلا بقدرة ، وإرادة ، ولا تتصور الإرادة إلا بعلم ، وقدرة الكتابة في اليد والأصابع ، والعلم بها ليس فى اليد ، إذ لا يزول بقطع اليد ، ولا إرادتها في اليد ، فإنه قد يريدها بعد شلل اليد ، وتتعذر لا لعدم الإرادة ، بل لعدم القدرة
So if someone says: Why do you not repel these proofs by [saying] that knowledge resides in the body in an indivisible, located substance, namely the indivisible atom? We say: Because discussion about the indivisible atom concerns geometrical matters, the resolution of which would be lengthy. Then there is nothing in it that repels the problem, for it would then be necessary that power and will also be in that part. For man has action, and that is conceivable only by power and will, and will is not conceivable except by knowledge. And the power of writing is in the hand and fingers, but knowledge of it is not in the hand, since it does not disappear with amputation of the hand, nor is the will to it in the hand, for one may will it after paralysis of the hand, and it becomes impossible not due to lack of will, but due to lack of power.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. The objector proposes that knowledge resides in an indivisible atom (jawhar fard) to avoid the problems of division. Ghazali responds that the atom is a geometrical concept with its own difficulties, and even if accepted, it does not solve the problem because power and will would also need to be in that atom, yet they are not localized in specific body parts. For example, knowledge of writing is not in the hand, as it remains after amputation, and the will to write is not in the hand, as one may will it after paralysis. Thus, the atom cannot be the locus of all mental faculties.

Key Terms

القدرة
الإرادة
العلم

D) The atom cannot be located in the body

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demonstrates that the indivisible atom cannot serve as the locus of knowledge because power and will are not localized in specific body parts, undermining the objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Knowledge of writing is not in your hand—it remains even if the hand is gone. How much do you attach your identity to your physical skills and appearance? If today you lost the use of your hand, would you still know who you are? What part of you is truly indispensable?
?
Ghazali argues that power, will, and knowledge cannot be in a single indivisible atom. But if these are not located in any part of your body, where do they come from? Do you truly believe that your will and knowledge are gifts from Allah, independent of the physical world? In your daily life, do you act as if your abilities are purely your own, or do you recognize their source?

Objection and Response on the Indivisible Substance
Ghazali responds to an objection invoking the indivisible substance (jawhar fard).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali rejects the proposal that knowledge resides in the indivisible substance (jawhar fard) and why this solution fails.

Core Idea
The indivisible substance does not solve the problem; it only shifts it to power and will.

This passage presents an objection to Ghazali's arguments about the location of knowledge. The objector suggests a potential solution by invoking the indivisible substance (jawhar fard). However, Ghazali argues that this proposal leads to further difficulties and does not resolve the original issue. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first line to grasp the objection: the objector proposes that knowledge resides in the indivisible substance (jawhar fard).
The objector assumes the existence of jawhar fard.
2
Ghazali's first response
Ghazali says that discussing the indivisible substance pertains to geometric matters and would be lengthy, and it does not remove the difficulty.
3
Ghazali's second response
He argues that if knowledge is in that part, then power (qudra) and will (irada) must also be there, leading to similar problems.
This is the core of his refutation.
4
Examine the example of writing
The power of writing is in the hand, but knowledge and will are not in the hand, as shown by amputation and paralysis.
This example illustrates the separation of power from knowledge and will.
5
Conclude why the objection fails
The indivisible substance does not resolve the issue; it merely relocates the problem.
Thus, the philosophers' position remains problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : هلا دفعتم هذه البراهين ، بأن العلم يحل من الجسم في جوهر متحيز لا يتجزأ ، وهو الجوهر الفرد ؟ ! قلنا : لأن الكلام فى الجوهر الفرد ، يتعلق بأمور هندسية ، يطول القول في حلها ثم ليس فيه ما يدفع الإشكال ، فإنه يلزم أن تكون القدرة والإرادة ، أيضاً فى ذلك الجزء ، فإن للإنسان فعلا ولا يتصور ذلك إلا بقدرة وإرادة ، ولا تتصور الإرادة إلا بعلم , وقدرة الكتابة في اليد والأصابع ، والعلم بها ليس فى اليد ، إذ لا يزول بقطع اليد ، ولا إرادتها في اليد ، فإنه قد يريدها بعد شلل اليد ، وتتعذر لا لعدم الإرادة ، بل لعدم القدرة
For someone might say: Why do you not refute these proofs by saying that knowledge resides in a body in a substance characterized by occupancy that is indivisible, namely the indivisible substance (al-jawhar al-fard)? We reply: Because the discussion about the indivisible substance pertains to geometric matters whose resolution would be lengthy. Moreover, there is nothing in it that removes the difficulty, for it would require that power (al-qudra) and will (al-irāda) also be in that part. Indeed, a human being performs an action, and that is only conceivable through power and will, and will is only conceivable through knowledge. And the power of writing is in the hand and fingers, but knowledge of it is not in the hand, since it does not disappear with the amputation of the hand. Nor is the will of it in the hand, for a person may intend it after paralysis of the hand, but it is impossible not due to absence of will, but due to absence of power.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer exchange. The objector proposes that the philosophers' difficulties about knowledge residing in a body could be avoided by positing the indivisible substance (jawhar fard) as the locus of knowledge. Ghazali gives two responses: first, discussion of the indivisible substance is lengthy and geometric; second, even if accepted, it would require that power and will also be in that part. For example, the power of writing is in the hand, but knowledge of writing is not in the hand (it remains after amputation), and the will to write persists even when the hand is paralyzed. Thus the indivisible substance does not solve the problem.

Key Terms
الجوهر الفرد
قدرة
إرادة
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The indivisible substance (jawhar fard) provides a simple and effective solution to the problem of where knowledge resides.
Correction: Ghazali shows that even if we accept the indivisible substance, it does not solve the problem because it would also require power and will to be located there, which leads to similar difficulties. Additionally, the discussion about it is lengthy and not directly relevant.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali reject the proposal that knowledge resides in the indivisible substance (jawhar fard)?
A) The indivisible substance does not exist.
B) The discussion is lengthy and it does not remove the difficulty, as it also requires power and will to be in that same part.
C) Knowledge is indivisible and cannot be located in any part.
D) The indivisible substance is not a body and cannot receive accidents.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali demonstrates that the indivisible substance does not salvage the philosophers' position because it merely relocates the problem without resolving it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever settled for a 'quick fix' to a deep spiritual doubt, hoping a simple answer would make the complexity disappear, rather than trusting Allah through the uncertainty? This lesson shows that even a learned concept like the indivisible substance fails to resolve the real issue. What question about your faith are you avoiding because it demands more wrestling than you want to give?
?
Where in your life do you rely on your own power, will, or knowledge as if they are independent of Allah? Do you truly believe that your ability to act, your intentions, and your understanding are all sustained by Him every moment? Let this lesson strip away any illusion of self-sufficiency.
?
When you lose a physical ability, like the power to speak or write, do you feel your worth or your connection to Allah diminish? The example of paralysis shows that will and knowledge remain even when power is gone. What does your identity rest on—your abilities or your relationship with the One who gives and takes them?

Causality and Created Things

The Fourth Proof: Knowledge and its Substrate (Philosophers' Argument)
Ghazali presents and critiques the philosophers' proof that knowledge has a single substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that knowledge and ignorance cannot reside in different parts of the same body, and how Ghazali initially refutes objections regarding the senses and metaphorical naming.

Core Idea
Knowledge and ignorance cannot coexist in different parts of the same body without absurdity.

The philosophers argue that if knowledge were physical, its opposite ignorance could occupy a different part, leading to a contradiction. Ghazali anticipates their reasoning and begins to dismantle it, starting with a crucial distinction between opposites and non-opposites.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' proof
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning up to where Ghazali addresses the senses. Notice the logical structure: if knowledge in a part → ignorance in another part → person both knowing and ignorant → impossible → therefore single locus.
This is the core of the argument.
2
Identify the key notion of opposite and locus
Focus on the terms 'ضد' (opposite) and 'محل' (locus). The proof hinges on the impossibility of opposites coexisting in the same substrate. Ghazali uses the example of piebaldness to illustrate coexistence in different loci.
Piebaldness: black and white in different parts of a horse.
3
See how Ghazali handles the senses and metaphors
Ghazali argues that the senses are not a counterexample because perceptions have no opposites. He also refutes the idea that 'knowing' applies to the whole body by explaining it as metaphorical, like saying 'He is in Baghdad' when only in part.
This strengthens the proof but also sets up Ghazali's eventual objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دلیل رابع
إن كان العلم يحل جزءاً من القلب أو الدماغ مثلا ، فالجهل ضده، فينبغى أن يجوز قيامه بجزء آخر من القلب أو الدماغ ، ويكون الإنسان في حال واحدة عالماً وجاهلاً بشيء واحد ، فلما استحال ذلك ، تبين أن محل الجهل ، هو محل العلم ، وأن ذلك المحل واحد ، يستحيل اجتماع الضدين فيه ، فإنه لو كان منقسما ، لما استحال قيام الجهل ببعضه ، والعلم ببعضه ، لأن الشيء في محل لا يضاده ضده ، في محل آخر ، كما يجتمع البلق، في الفرس الواحد والسواد والبياض فى العين الواحدة ، ولكن في محلين
ولا يلزم هذا فى الحواس ، فإنه لا ضد لإدراكاتها ، ولكنه قد يدرك وقد لا يدرك ، فليس بينهما ألا تقابل الوجود والعدم، فلا جرم نقول : يدرك ببعض أجزائه كالعين والأذن ، ولا يدرك بسائر بدنه ، وليس فيه تناقض .
ولا يغنى عن هذا قولكم : إن العالمية مضادة للجاهلية ، والحكم عام لجميع البدن ، إذ يستحيل أن يكون الحكم في غير محل العلة ، فالعالم هو المحل الذي قام العلم به ، فإن أطلق الاسم على الجملة فبالمجاز ، كما يقال : هو في بغداد وإن كان هو في بعضها ، وكما يقال : هو مبصر ، وإن كنا بالضرورة نعلم ، أن حكم الإبصار لا يثبت للرجل واليد ، بل يختص بالعين ، وتضاد الأحكام كتضاد العلل ، فإن الأحكام تقتصر على محل العلل
ولا يخلص من هذا قول القائل : إن المحل المهيأ لقبول العلم والجهل من الإنسان واحد ، فيتضادان عليه ، فإن عندكم أن كل جسم فيه حياة فهو قابل للعلم والجهل ، ولم تشترطوا سوى الحياة ، شريطة أخرى ، وسائر أجزاء البدن عندكم في قبول العلم على وتيرة واحدة
الاعتراض: أن هذا ينقلب عليكم فى الشهوة والشوق والإرادة ، فإن هذه الأمور تثبت للبهائم والإنسان ، وهى معان تنطبع في الجسم ، ثم يستحيل أن ينفر عما يشتاق إليه ، فيجتمع فيه النفرة والميل إلى شيء واحد ، بوجود الشوق في محل ، والنفرة في محل آخر ، وذلك لا يدل على أنها لا تحل الأجسام ، وذلك لأن هذه القوى وإن كانت كثيرة ، ومتوزعة على آلات مختلفة ، فلها رابطة واحدة وهى النفس ، وذلك للبهيمة والإنسان جميعاً ، وإذا اتحدت الرابطة ، استحالت الإضافات المتناقضة ، بالنسبة إليها ، وهذا لا يدل على كون النفس ، غير منطبعة في الجسم ، كما في البهائم .
Fourth Proof: If knowledge (al-ilm) were to reside in a part of the heart or the brain, for example, then ignorance (al-jahl) is its opposite; it would be permissible for it to subsist in another part of the heart or brain, and a person would be at one and the same time knowing and ignorant of one thing. Since that is impossible, it becomes clear that the locus (mahall) of ignorance is the locus of knowledge, and that locus is one; it is impossible for two opposites to coexist in it. For if it were divided, the subsistence of ignorance in some of it and knowledge in some of it would not be impossible, because a thing in a locus is not opposed by its opposite in another locus, just as black-and-white-piebaldness (al-balaq) coexists in one horse, and black and white in one eye, but in two loci. And this does not apply to the senses, for there is no opposite to their perceptions; rather, it may perceive or not perceive. There is only the opposition of existence and non-existence. So we say without hesitation: it perceives by some of its parts, like the eye and ear, and does not perceive by the rest of its body, and there is no contradiction in that. And your saying does not suffice: that being knowing (al-ālimiyya) is opposite to being ignorant (al-jāhiliyya), and the judgment applies generally to the whole body, for it is impossible for the judgment to be in a locus other than that of the cause (al-illa). The knower (al-ālim) is the locus in which knowledge subsists; if the name is applied to the whole, it is by way of metaphor (al-majāz), just as it is said: 'He is in Baghdad' although he is only in part of it, and as it is said: 'He is seeing,' although we know by necessity that the judgment of sight does not hold for the leg or hand, but is specific to the eye. The oppositions of judgments are like the oppositions of causes, for judgments are confined to the locus of the causes. And the saying of the one who says does not deliver from this: that the locus prepared for receiving knowledge and ignorance in man is one, so they are opposites with respect to it. For according to you, every body that has life is receptive to knowledge and ignorance, and you stipulate nothing besides life as an additional condition. And all parts of the body, according to you, are on a single level in receiving knowledge. Objection (al-iʿtirāḍ): This argument turns against you concerning desire (al-shahwa), longing (al-shawq), and will (al-irāda). For these things are established in beasts and man; they are meanings (maʿānin) that are impressed in the body. Then it is impossible for one to be averse to what he longs for, such that aversion and inclination toward one thing would coexist in him by the existence of longing in one locus and aversion in another locus. That does not indicate that they do not reside in bodies. That is because these powers, even though they are many and distributed among different instruments, have a single bond (rābiṭa) which is the soul (al-nafs). That applies to both beast and man. And when the bond is one, contradictory relations (al-iḍāfāt al-mutanāqiḍa) become impossible with respect to it. This does not indicate that the soul is not impressed in the body, as in the case of beasts.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof of the philosophers, followed by Ghazali's objection. The proof begins by positing that if knowledge (al-ilm) were to occupy a physical part, say a part of the heart or brain, then its opposite, ignorance (al-jahl), could similarly occupy another part. This would allow a person to be simultaneously knowing and ignorant of the same thing, which is impossible. Hence, the locus of knowledge and ignorance must be one and the same; we cannot have them in different parts because opposites cannot coexist in the same locus if that locus is single. If the locus were divided, it would be possible for opposites to be in different parts, just as piebaldness (black and white patches) exists in one horse or black and white in one eye but in separate loci. Ghazali then addresses a potential objection regarding the senses: sense perceptions have no opposites (they only have existence and non-existence), so it is not contradictory that one perceives by some parts (like the eye) and not by others. The philosophers may argue that the attribute 'being knowing' is opposite to 'being ignorant' and that the judgment applies to the whole body. Ghazali refutes this by saying that the knower is the actual locus where knowledge subsists; applying the name to the whole body is metaphorical, just as saying 'He is in Baghdad' when he is only in part of it, or calling a person 'seeing' when only the eye sees. Judgments are confined to the locus of their causes. Another possible counterargument is that the prepared locus for knowledge and ignorance is one; but Ghazali notes that according to the philosophers, any living body is equally receptive to knowledge, so they cannot claim a single locus without an additional condition. Finally, Ghazali presents his objection (al-iʿtirāḍ): the same reasoning applies to desire, longing, and will, which are impressed in the body. If these can coexist in different parts (e.g., longing in one part, aversion in another) without contradiction because they are held together by a single bond (the soul), then why can't knowledge and ignorance also be in different parts? This shows that the philosophers' proof does not necessarily lead to a single locus for knowledge, and it does not disprove that the soul is impressed in the body.

Key Terms
علم (ʿilm) knowledge
جهل (jahl) ignorance
ضد (ḍidd) opposite
محل (maḥall) locus
علة (ʿilla) cause
عالم (ʿālim) knower
إدراك (idrāk) perception

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Opposites like knowledge and ignorance can coexist in different parts of the same body without contradiction.
Correction: The philosophers argue this would lead to a person being simultaneously knowing and ignorant of the same thing, which is logically impossible.

Checkpoint
Why does the philosophers' proof conclude that knowledge and ignorance must share a single locus?
A) Because the heart and brain are indivisible.
B) Because otherwise a person would be both knowing and ignorant of the same thing at once.
C) Because ignorance is not a real quality.
D) Because the philosophers deny the possibility of ignorance.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' proof that knowledge must have a single substrate relies on the impossibility of opposites coexisting in the same person, but Ghazali's handling of senses and metaphor shows the argument's complexity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there areas in your life where you claim to know something (like a religious truth) but your actions reveal ignorance? What would it mean for your heart to be both knowing and ignorant of that same truth? Would you be willing to let one of them go?
?
If true knowledge resides in a single, undivided locus of your soul, how can you justify harboring contradictory beliefs or doubts about fundamental matters of faith? What would it take to bring your heart to complete unity on this issue?
?
When you say 'I know' about a matter of Aqeedah, is it a truth that has taken root in the core of your being, or is it merely a label applied to you? How can you ensure that knowledge truly subsists in you and is not just a metaphorical attribution?

Ghazali's Objection: Desire, Longing, and the Soul
Ghazali presents a counterexample from desire and aversion to refute the philosophers' proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's objection that the philosophers' argument about a single substrate for knowledge backfires when applied to desires and aversions, which coexist due to the soul as a single bond.

Core Idea
The same logic used by the philosophers can be turned against them using desire and aversion.

Ghazali now turns the tables on the philosophers. He points out that desires and aversions are opposites that reside in the body, yet they can coexist without contradiction because of the soul's unifying function. This undermines the philosophers' claim that knowledge must have a single physical substrate.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully. Ghazali argues that desire (shahwa), longing (shawq), and will (irada) are meanings impressed in the body. They can have opposites (aversion) that coexist in different parts, yet the person is not contradictory because the soul unites them.
This mirrors the structure of the philosophers' proof but reaches a different conclusion.
2
See how it mirrors the proof
Note the parallel: just as knowledge and ignorance were said to require a single locus, desire and aversion also seem to require a single locus. But Ghazali shows they can be in different parts and still be unified by the soul.
This is a direct rebuttal showing the proof is not universally valid.
3
Grasp the role of the soul
The soul acts as a single bond (rābiṭa) that ties together multiple loci. This allows opposite relations to coexist without contradiction. Ghazali uses this to argue that the same could apply to knowledge.
This does not prove the soul is separate from the body; it can still be impressed in the body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الاعتراض: أن هذا ينقلب عليكم فى الشهوة والشوق والإرادة ، فإن هذه الأمور تثبت للبهائم والإنسان ، وهى معان تنطبع في الجسم ، ثم يستحيل أن ينفر عما يشتاق إليه ، فيجتمع فيه النفرة والميل إلى شيء واحد ، بوجود الشوق في محل ، والنفرة في محل آخر ، وذلك لا يدل على أنها لا تحل الأجسام ، وذلك لأن هذه القوى وإن كانت كثيرة ، ومتوزعة على آلات مختلفة ، فلها رابطة واحدة وهى النفس ، وذلك للبهيمة والإنسان جميعاً ، وإذا اتحدت الرابطة ، استحالت الإضافات المتناقضة ، بالنسبة إليها ، وهذا لا يدل على كون النفس ، غير منطبعة في الجسم ، كما في البهائم .
Fourth Proof: If knowledge (al-ilm) were to reside in a part of the heart or the brain, for example, then ignorance (al-jahl) is its opposite; it would be permissible for it to subsist in another part of the heart or brain, and a person would be at one and the same time knowing and ignorant of one thing. So we say without hesitation: it perceives by some of its parts, like the eye and ear, and does not perceive by the rest of its body, and there is no contradiction in that. The oppositions of judgments are like the oppositions of causes, for judgments are confined to the locus of the causes. And the saying of the one who says does not deliver from this: that the locus prepared for receiving knowledge and ignorance in man is one, so they are opposites with respect to it. For according to you, every body that has life is receptive to knowledge and ignorance, and you stipulate nothing besides life as an additional condition. And all parts of the body, according to you, are on a single level in receiving knowledge. Objection (al-iʿtirāḍ): This argument turns against you concerning desire (al-shahwa), longing (al-shawq), and will (al-irāda). For these things are established in beasts and man; they are meanings (maʿānin) that are impressed in the body. Then it is impossible for one to be averse to what he longs for, such that aversion and inclination toward one thing would coexist in him by the existence of longing in one locus and aversion in another locus. That does not indicate that they do not reside in bodies. That is because these powers, even though they are many and distributed among different instruments, have a single bond (rābiṭa) which is the soul (al-nafs). That applies to both beast and man. And when the bond is one, contradictory relations (al-iḍāfāt al-mutanāqiḍa) become impossible with respect to it. Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof of the philosophers, followed by Ghazali's objection. The proof begins by positing that if knowledge (al-ilm) were to occupy a physical part, say a part of the heart or brain, then its opposite, ignorance (al-jahl), could similarly occupy another part. This would allow a person to be simultaneously knowing and ignorant of the same thing, which is impossible. Hence, the locus of knowledge and ignorance must be one and the same; we cannot have them in different parts because opposites cannot coexist in the same locus if that locus is single. If the locus were divided, it would be possible for opposites to be in different parts, just as piebaldness (black and white patches) exists in one horse or black and white in one eye but in separate loci. Ghazali then addresses a potential objection regarding the senses: sense perceptions have no opposites (they only have existence and non-existence), so it is not contradictory that one perceives by some parts (like the eye) and not by others. Judgments are confined to the locus of their causes. Another possible counterargument is that the prepared locus for knowledge and ignorance is one; but Ghazali notes that according to the philosophers, any living body is equally receptive to knowledge, so they cannot claim a single locus without an additional condition. Finally, Ghazali presents his objection (al-iʿtirāḍ): the same reasoning applies to desire, longing, and will, which are impressed in the body. If these can coexist in different parts (e.g., longing in one part, aversion in another) without contradiction because they are held together by a single bond (the soul), then why can't knowledge and ignorance also be in different parts? This shows that the philosophers' proof does not necessarily lead to a single locus for knowledge, and it does not disprove that the soul is impressed in the body.

Key Terms
شوق (shawq) longing
شهوة (shahwa) desire
إرادة (irāda) will
نفس (nafs) soul
إضافات متناقضة (iḍāfāt mutanāqiḍa) contradictory relations

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' argument about knowledge is universally valid and cannot be refuted by other examples.
Correction: Ghazali shows that the same logical structure can be applied to desires, which are also opposites, but they coexist in different parts without contradiction due to the soul's unity.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Ghazali's objection?
A) Desires cannot be opposites.
B) The philosophers' argument fails because similar opposites (desire/aversion) can coexist in different parts united by a single soul.
C) The soul is immaterial and cannot be in the body.
D) Knowledge is not really an attribute.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's objection shows that the philosophers' proof is not conclusive, because opposite attributes can coexist in different parts if a unifying principle like the soul exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just seen that Ghazali uses desire and aversion as a counterexample to a purely rational proof. Think of a time when you experienced conflicting desires—e.g., wanting to please Allah but also feeling drawn to a sin. Did you resolve that conflict by recognizing a deeper unifying principle in your heart? What would change if you actively submitted all your conflicting inclinations to the soul's true purpose: obedience to Allah?
?
The philosophers thought their logic was unassailable, but Ghazali showed it could be turned against them. In your own life, are there areas where you have placed too much confidence in your own reasoning or in what 'makes sense' to you, ignoring the possibility that your limited understanding might be wrong? How can this lesson humble you before the revelation?
?
Ghazali ends by noting that the soul could still be impressed in the body, as in animals. If your soul is a single bond unifying your actions, are you aware of how your desires and aversions are being shaped? Which desires are you feeding, and which are you starving? What does that reveal about the state of your soul today?

Fifth Proof: The Intellect Knowing Itself and Ghazali's Refutation
Philosophers' proof and Ghazali's refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that the intellect's self-awareness proves it is non-bodily, and Ghazali's critique that the logical entailment and analogy to the senses are not necessarily valid.

Core Idea
Self-awareness does not automatically prove immateriality; the inference depends on an unproven link.

The philosophers present a proof that the intellect cannot be a body because it knows itself, while bodily instruments like the eye cannot perceive themselves. Ghazali responds by granting the logical form but challenging whether the entailment from ‘bodily instrument’ to ‘cannot know itself’ is actually established. He also questions the force of the sensory analogy. This lesson walks through the proof and the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conditional proof
Read the philosophers' statement: If the intellect uses a bodily instrument, then it cannot know itself. But it does know itself, so it does not use a bodily instrument.
Notice the logical structure: modus tollens.
2
Examine the analogy to the senses
The philosophers defend the conditional by analogy: sight does not see itself, hearing does not hear itself, etc. So if intellect were bodily, it would not know itself.
Consider whether the analogy is strong.
3
Follow Ghazali's response
Ghazali first acknowledges the logical form but then denies that the entailment is established. He asks for evidence that a bodily instrument prevents self-intellection.
Ghazali's move is to shift the burden of proof.
4
Reflect on the limits of analogy
The philosophers' analogy to senses might not apply because intellect is different from physical senses.
This is Ghazali's implicit critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دلیل خامس
قولهم : إن كان العقل يدرك بآلة جسمانية ، فهو لا يعقل نفسه ، والتالى محال ، لأنه يعقل نفسه ، فالمقدم محال
قلنا : مسلم أن « استثناء نقيض التالي » ، ينتج « نقيض المقدم » ، ولكن إذا ثبت اللزوم بين التالي والمقدم ، بل نقول : لا يسلم لزوم التالى للمقدم وما الدليل عليه
فإن قيل : الدليل عليه أن الإبصار لما كان بجسم ، فالإبصار لا يتعلق بالإبصار فالرؤية لا ترى ، والسمع لا يسمع ، وكذا سائر الحواس ، فإن كان العقل أيضاً لا يدرك إلا بجسم ، فلا يدرك نفسه ، والعقل كما يعقل غيره ، يعقل نفسه ، فإن الواحد منا ، كما يعقل غيره ، يعقل نفسه ، ويعقل أنه عقل غيره ، وأنه عقل نفسه
Fifth Proof. Their saying: If the intellect (al-ʿaql) perceives by means of a bodily instrument (āla jismāniyya), then it does not intellect itself (lā yaʿqilu nafsahu). The consequent (al-tālī) is impossible, because it does intellect itself. So the antecedent (al-muqaddam) is impossible. We say: It is granted that 'the exclusion of the contradictory of the consequent' yields 'the contradictory of the antecedent', but only if the entailment (al-luzūm) between the consequent and the antecedent is established. Rather, we say: The entailment of the consequent from the antecedent is not conceded. What is the evidence for it? If it is said: The evidence for it is that since sight (al-ibṣār) is by a body, sight does not pertain to sight; vision does not see itself, hearing does not hear itself, and likewise the rest of the senses. So if the intellect also perceives only by a body, then it does not perceive itself. Yet the intellect, just as it intellects other things, intellects itself. Indeed, each one of us, just as he intellects other things, intellects himself, and intellects that he intellects other things and that he intellects himself.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof of the philosophers and Ghazali's refutation. The philosophers argue: If the intellect (al-ʿaql) perceives by means of a bodily instrument, then it cannot intellect itself (because bodily instruments like the eye cannot perceive themselves). But the intellect does intellect itself (since we are aware of our own thinking). Therefore, the intellect cannot be using a bodily instrument; it must be non-physical. Ghazali responds by first acknowledging the logical form: if the antecedent implies the consequent, then denying the consequent (which is impossible) leads to denying the antecedent. However, Ghazali challenges the entailment itself: why must it be that a bodily instrument prevents self-intellectual? He questions the evidence. The philosophers then provide the analogy: sight does not see itself, hearing does not hear itself, and so on for the senses. Therefore, if intellect were bodily, it would follow that it does not intellect itself. But Ghazali implies that this analogy is not necessarily valid; the intellect might be different from the senses. The passage ends with the philosophers' assertion that we do indeed intellect ourselves and our own intellection, but Ghazali does not concede the entailment.

عقل (ʿaql) intellect
آلة جسمانية (āla jismāniyya) bodily instrument
يعقل نفسه (yaʿqilu nafsahu) intellects itself
مقدم (muqaddam) antecedent
تالي (tālī) consequent
لزوم (luzūm) entailment
إبصار (ibṣār) sight
إدراك (idrāk) perception

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The intellect's self-awareness automatically proves it is non-physical.
Correction: Ghazali shows that the inference depends on the unproven claim that a bodily instrument prevents self-intellection. The analogy to the senses is not necessarily valid for the intellect.

Checkpoint
What is the philosophers' key analogy in the fifth proof?
A) The intellect is like a mirror that reflects itself.
B) Sight does not see itself, so if intellect is bodily, it cannot know itself.
C) The hand cannot touch itself, so the intellect cannot know itself.
D) The heart feels itself, so the intellect must be in the heart.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali challenges the necessary connection between bodily nature and inability for self-knowledge, showing that the philosophers' proof is not decisive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You experience self-awareness every moment. Have you ever assumed that this proves you are more than a physical body? How does Ghazali's challenge unsettle that assumption, and what does that mean for how you understand your own existence?
?
The philosophers used an analogy from the senses to argue about the intellect. When have you assumed that what is true for one part of your experience applies to another? Are you willing to question those analogies, even when they seem obvious?
?
Ghazali does not reject the conclusion that the intellect is non-physical, only the proof. How does this affect your trust in arguments that feel logically compelling? Can you think of a belief you hold for which you have not examined the underlying 'entailment'?

Refuting Induction from the Senses with the Crocodile Analogy
Ghazali refutes the philosophers' inductive argument from the senses using the crocodile analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the crocodile analogy to refute the philosophers' inductive argument for the physicality of the intellect.

Core Idea
Incomplete induction can fail; the intellect may be an exception like the crocodile.

Ghazali challenges the philosophers' claim that since all known perception occurs through bodily senses, the intellect must also be bodily. He exposes the logical flaw in their inductive reasoning by drawing a powerful analogy from logic itself: the crocodile. This passage shows how a single overlooked exception can undermine a universal claim, and invites us to consider that the intellect might be a different kind of perceiver.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the flow of Ghazali's argument.
The Arabic is dense; the translation helps clarify.
2
Identify the philosophers' argument
The philosophers argue that because the five senses are corporeal and perceive, the intellect, being a perceiver, must also be corporeal. Find where Ghazali states this.
Look for references to 'الحواس الخمس'.
3
Understand the crocodile analogy
Ghazali gives an example from logic: a man who claims all animals move their lower jaw because he has seen many do so, but he is ignorant of the crocodile. This shows that incomplete induction can miss exceptions. Apply this to the philosophers: they may have missed the intellect as an exception.
The crocodile is an exception among animals.
4
Consider the division of senses
Ghazali then notes that even among corporeal senses, there is variety: some perceive their own locus, some don't; some perceive by contact, some without. This further weakens the generalization.
This shows diversity within the senses themselves.
5
Conclude on certainty
The passage ends by stating that the philosophers' argument at best yields probability, not certainty. Reflect on why Ghazali insists on certainty in theological matters.
Certainty is required for metaphysical claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يستحيل أن يدرك الجسم الذي هو محله ، لم يلزم أن يحكم من جزئى معين ، على كلى مرسل ؟ ! بسبب جزئی ومما عرف بالاتفاق بطلانه ، وذكر في المنطق ، أن يحكم أو جزئيات كثيرة ، على كلى ، حتى مثلوه بما إذا قال الإنسان : إن كل حيوان فإنه يحرك عند المضغ فكه الأسفل، لأنا استقرأنا الحيوانات كلها فرأيناها كذلك ، فيكون ذلك لغفلته عن « التمساح » فإنه يحرك فكه الأعلى . وهؤلاء لم يستقرئوا إلا الحواس الخمس ، فوجدوها على وجه معلوم ، فحكموا على الكل به ، فلعل العقل حاسة أخرى ، تجرى من سائر الحواس ، مجرى التمساح من سائر الحيوانات ، فتكون إذن الحواس ، مع كونها جسمانية ، منقسمة إلى : ما تدرك محلها ، وإلى ما لا تدرك ، كما انقسمت إلى ما يدرك مدركه ، من غير مماسة كالبصر ، وإلى ما لا يدرك إلا بالاتصال كالذوق واللمس . فما ذكروه أيضاً ، إن أورث ظناً ، فلا يورث يقيناً موثوقاً به .
It is impossible... if it perceives the body which is its locus, it does not necessarily judge from a particular individual to a universal absolute? Because of a particular? And it is known by agreement to be invalid, and it is mentioned in logic that one judges from many particulars to a universal, to the extent that they gave an example: when a man says, 'Every animal moves its lower jaw when chewing, because we have investigated all animals and found them so,' this is due to his ignorance of the crocodile, for it moves its upper jaw. These people investigated only the five senses, found them in a known manner, and judged the whole accordingly. Perhaps the intellect is another sense, with respect to the other senses like the crocodile with respect to other animals. So the senses, despite being corporeal, would then be divided into those that perceive their locus and those that do not, just as they are divided into those that perceive their object without contact, like sight, and those that perceive only through contact, like taste and touch. What they mentioned, even if it produces probability, does not produce certain reliable knowledge.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' inductive argument that because all known perceptions occur through bodily senses, the intellect must also be bodily. Ghazali first points out the logical flaw in arguing from a particular to a universal without exhaustive induction, citing the example from logic of the man who erroneously claims all animals move their lower jaw, ignoring the crocodile. He then applies this to the philosophers: they only investigated the five senses and generalized to all perception, but perhaps the intellect is a sixth sense unlike the others. Just as the crocodile is an exception among animals, the intellect may be an exception among perceivers. Furthermore, even among corporeal senses, there is variety: some perceive their own locus, some do not; some perceive by contact, some without. Therefore, the philosophers' argument at best yields probability, not certainty.

Key Terms
الاستقراء
الحواس
العقل
التمساح
اليقين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali denies all inductive reasoning.
Correction: Ghazali does not reject induction per se; he rejects incomplete induction used as certain proof for metaphysical claims, as illustrated by the crocodile example.

Checkpoint
What does the example of the crocodile illustrate in Ghazali's argument?
A) That crocodiles are unique among animals.
B) That generalization from incomplete induction can fail due to exceptions.
C) That the senses are corporeal.
D) That the intellect is like the crocodile.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' inductive argument from the senses is not certain because it may overlook an exception, just as the crocodile is an exception among animals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life have you drawn a conclusion based on limited evidence, assuming your experience covers all possibilities? How will you seek to test that assumption today?
?
Have you ever dismissed a perspective or possibility because it didn't fit your previous pattern, like the crocodile being overlooked? What might you be missing in your understanding of others or of Allah's creation?
?
Ghazali warns that even a well-supported inductive argument only yields probability, not certainty. Are there beliefs you hold that you have not examined for their certainty? Will you commit to seeking yaqin in your faith and actions?

Seventh Proof: Bodily Powers Tire but Intellect Does Not
Ghazali presents the philosophers' premise that bodily senses fatigue, with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that bodily perceptual powers fatigue, and how this premise is used to support the non-physicality of the intellect.

Core Idea
Bodily fatigue points to a non-physical intellect.

In this passage, Ghazali begins presenting the seventh proof of the philosophers, who argue that the intellect must be non-physical because it does not suffer from fatigue. They compare it to bodily senses that tire from continuous or intense use. This lesson unpacks that initial premise, setting the stage for the full argument and Ghazali's eventual refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: a general claim, an explanation, then examples.
The passage is incomplete; the conclusion is not included.
2
Identify the key claim
Find where the philosophers state that perceptual powers using bodily instruments are subject to fatigue. Underline that sentence.
3
Understand the reasoning
Why does fatigue occur? According to the passage, continuous motion corrupts the temperament (mizaj) of bodies. Also, strong and clear objects of perception weaken or corrupt the sense.
These are two distinct mechanisms.
4
Reflect on the examples
The passage gives examples: a loud sound for hearing, and a strong light for vision (implied). Think about how these illustrate the premise.
5
Consider the missing conclusion
Since the passage is cut off, imagine how the argument continues: if bodily senses fatigue but the intellect does not tire from prolonged thinking, then the intellect must be non-physical.
Ghazali will refute this later in the book.

Likewise, strong and clear objects of perception weaken them and perhaps corrupt them, so that after them they do not perceive weaker and fainter things, like a loud sound for hearing and strong light
